New York then was several different cities. One New York was made up of people who worked for a living. The other was much smaller. It was made up of families who were so rich they did not need to work.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nEdith was born into the wealthy New York. In one of her childhood stories, a woman apologizes for not having a completely clean house when another woman makes an unexpected visit. Edith\'s mother read the story. To do this, however, she would have had to remove herself from the group to see it clearly. She could not do this, even intellectually. Her education and her traditions made it impossible.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nThe subject of Edith Wharton\'s writing became the story of the young and innocent in a dishonest world. This idea is central to much of Edith Wharton\'s best writing. The old families of New York are in conflict with the newly rich families. But the New York she wrote about was different from the New York of those who came after her.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nEdith Wharton was born in New York City in eighteen sixty-two. New York then was several different cities. One New York was made up of people who worked for a living. The other was much smaller. It was made up of families who were so rich they did not need to work.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nEdith was born into the wealthy New York. But there was a &quot;right&quot; wealthy New York and a &quot;wrong&quot; wealthy New York. Among the rich there were those who had been given money by parents or grandparents. It was a group that did not want its way of living changed. It also was a group without many ideas of its own. It was from this group that Edith Wharton created herself.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nDOUG JOHNSON: Like many girls her age, Edith wrote stories. In one of her childhood stories, a woman apologizes for not having a completely clean house when another woman makes an unexpected visit. Edith\'s mother read the story. Her only comment was that one\'s house was always clean and ready for visitors. Edith\'s house always was.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nEdith spent much of her childhood in Europe. She had an international social life and left a large collection of letters.<br />\r\nIn her lifetime she published about fifty books on a number of subjects.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nMany critics believe Edith Wharton should have written the story of her social group. To do this, however, she would have had to remove herself from the group to see it clearly. She could not do this, even intellectually. Her education and her traditions made it impossible.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nThe subject of Edith Wharton\'s writing became the story of the young and innocent in a dishonest world. She did not make a connection between her work and her own life. What she had was the ability to speak plainly about emotions that, until then, had been hidden.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nShe also was among the first American women writers to gain a sense of the world as an evil place. &quot;Life is the saddest thing,&quot; she wrote, &quot;next to death.&quot;<br />\r\n<br />\r\n(MUSIC)<br />\r\n<br />\r\nPHOEBE ZIMMERMANN: To show that she could do more than just write stories, she wrote a book with Ogden Codman, &ldquo;The Decoration of Houses.&rdquo; It was very successful. About the same time, her poems and stories also began to be published in Scribner\'s Magazine.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nIn eighteen ninety-nine her collection of stories, &ldquo;The Greater Inclination,&rdquo; appeared. He said to her, &quot;This is what everyone in London is talking about now.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nDOUG JOHNSON: Three years later her first novel, &ldquo;The Valley of Decision,&rdquo; was published. As in so many stories by Edith Wharton, the main character does not control what happens to her. She is a victim who is defeated by forces she does not fight to overcome. This idea is central to much of Edith Wharton\'s best writing. The old families of New York are in conflict with the newly rich families. The major people in the stories are trapped in a hopeless struggle with social forces more powerful than they. And they struggle against people whose beliefs and actions are not as moral as theirs.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nPHOEBE ZIMMERMANN: This is the situation in one of Wharton\'s most popular books, &ldquo;Ethan Frome,&rdquo; published in nineteen eleven. This means one of two things. \n      Either he hid the problem from his trainers and coaches. Or less severe \n      hits can lead to C-T-E.<br>\n      <br>\n      Researchers say his death should be a warning to other athletes. \n      Concussions are not like most injuries. They can become life-threatening, \n      but cannot be seen.<br>\n      <br>\n      KATHERINE COLE: America\'s Centers for Disease Control and Prevention \n      receives reports of about four million sports-related concussions every \n      year. Concussions can result when an athlete is hit in the head. The brain \n      will move around and sometimes strike the skull, or bony inside of the \n      head. This can result in swelling. In extreme cases, it causes bleeding in \n      the brain.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021172648.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          A 2007 \n          photo of Chris Henry</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      After suffering a concussion, \n      a player might feel light-headed and pass out. He or she may also have \n      problems with memory and feel sick to the stomach. Researchers are not \n      saying that no one should play contact sports. They just want athletes to \n      know that head injuries are serious. And, repeated head injuries are cause \n      for concern.<br>\n      <br>\n      The best thing to do is seek medical attention. This way doctors can \n      document the injury before it becomes too late.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      BOB DOUGHTY: American teenagers hear it all the time from their parents. \n      “Are you listening to me? Turn down that music or you will hurt your \n      ears.” A new study has found the parents may be right. The Journal of the \n      American Medical Association published results of the study last month.<br>\n      <br>\n      The study found that many young people in the United States do not hear as \n      well as they used to. One out of five American teenagers has some hearing \n      loss. That is a thirty percent increase in the past fifteen years. One out \n      of twenty teens has a large hearing loss. That is a fifty percent \n      increase.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021172649.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          Matthew \n          Brady, 17, of Foxborough, Massachusetts has some mild hearing loss \n          from listening to loud music on his earphones</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      KATHERINE COLE: The study \n      used results of hearing tests taken from nineteen eighty-eight through \n      nineteen ninety-four. The researchers compared that information with \n      studies done in two thousand five and two thousand six.<br>\n      <br>\n      They found that boys are more likely to have hearing problems than girls. \n      The researchers say that teenagers from poor families had more hearing \n      loss than those from more wealthy families. The age or race of the teens \n      who were tested had no effect on their chances for hearing loss.<br>\n      <br>\n      BOB DOUGHTY: The researchers say they are not sure why some teenagers are \n      losing their hearing. Not enough studies have been done and more are \n      needed. Some say the causes may be found in their diet, exposure to \n      poisons, or in their genes. Others believe that listening to loud music on \n      mp3 players with earphones is the major cause.<br>\n      <br>\n      Researchers think that some teenagers do not realize that they are \n      listening to music at very loud levels. To them, loud rock music may seem \n      normal.<br>\n      <br>\n      KATHERINE COLE: Ron Eavey is head of the Department of Otolaryngology at \n      the Vanderbilt Bill Wilkerson Center. He says that parents should stand \n      next to their children while they are wearing earphones. If the parents \n      can hear the music, the sound is too loud. He says that teenagers should \n      set the volume control on their music players to somewhere between \n      one-half and two-thirds the highest level, never louder.<br>\n      <br>\n      Even a small hearing loss can affect a child’s social development. \n      Communication skills and grades in school can also suffer. Doctor Eavey \n      says, “What we’re seeing is a big jump in the prevalence of hearing loss \n      in a very short time, in less than one generation.”<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      BOB DOUGHTY: Finally, an international study has shown that scientists in \n      Denmark are the happiest with their work. The journal &quot;Nature&quot; published \n      the findings.<br>\n      <br>\n      More than ten thousand five hundred scientists rated their satisfaction \n      with issues like their pay, health care and retirement plan. Sixteen \n      countries provided large enough sample sizes for study organizers to \n      comment on the findings. They said Denmark seems to be the most likely \n      place for a fine research experience. Other countries where scientists \n      seemed happiest were the Netherlands, Sweden, France and Switzerland.<br>\n      <br>\n      KATHERINE COLE: In the study, those questioned rated themselves. One area \n      they judged was the amount of independence they had in their work. They \n      also rated the guidance they received from other workers and more \n      experienced researchers. The study showed a big \n      difference between money paid to scientists in private industry as \n      compared with universities. Those in private industry received much more.<br>\n      <br>\n      Scientists in Japan who answered the study expressed the least \n      satisfaction. Only a small percentage said they were very satisfied. Two \n      percent thought their retirement plan was good. Just ten percent thought \n      they had enough independence to do their work.<br>\n      <br>\n      BOB DOUGHTY: Scientists in India and China also reported low levels of job \n      satisfaction. Many banks did not have enough money in reserve to \n      protect against their losses. Now there is a proposed solution.<br>\n      <br>\n      On Sunday, banking supervisors from twenty-six nations and Hong Kong met \n      in Basel, Switzerland. They announced proposals to make banks safer by \n      requiring them to increase their reserves.<br>\n      <br>\n      The Basel Committee on Banking Supervision has been working on a set of \n      recommendations known as Basel Three. These are based on agreements \n      reached in July by officials from a group of leading industrial nations.<br>\n      <br>\n      The goal is to stop the cycle of easing rules on banks in good times and \n      tightening them only after a crisis.<br>\n      <br>\n      Under the new rules, banks would have to hold reserves equal to seven \n      percent of their risk-weighted assets. Mainly this means loans. Currently \n      banks are required to hold two percent in reserve.<br>\n      <br>\n      The bigger reserves could be in the form of cash or common stock, also \n      known as common equity. Banks would also have to hold extra reserves as \n      their national economies improve.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021172231.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"113\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          The \n          Federal Reserve in Washington is the United States\' central bank. \n          International bank supervisors want banks to increase their capital \n          reserves for a rainy day</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      The new requirements would go \n      into effect starting in January of two thousand thirteen. Banks would have \n      five years to fully meet them. International banking lawyer Ernie Patrikis, \n      a former vice president of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York, explains \n      why.<br>\n      <br>\n      ERNIE PATRIKIS: &quot;We cannot be telling banks, on the one hand, raise \n      capital right away, and on the other hand lend more.&quot;<br>\n      <br>\n      Economic growth remains slow in much of the developed world. The worry is \n      that any decrease in lending could hurt a global recovery.<br>\n      <br>\n      One way for banks to meet the proposed new rules would be to sell more of \n      their stock. That is what Germany’s Deutsche Bank did last week. It \n      announced a sale offer valued at over eleven billion dollars.<br>\n      <br>\n      Ernie Patrikis thinks chief executive officers of banks have three \n      choices.<br>\n      <br>\n      ERNIE PATRIKIS: &quot;One is to go out and raise more common equity. Another \n      one is to not pay dividends. And that’s not something most CEO’s want to \n      do because their shareholders aren’t going to be particularly happy. And \n      the third choice is sell assets -- downsize the bank.&quot;<br>\n      <br>\n      Nations on the Basel committee will now seek to pass the new rules into \n      law so their banking supervisors can enforce them.<br>\n      <br>\n      Banks that fall below the reserve limits could have to stop paying \n      dividends to shareholders or bonuses to top employees. Ending dividends \n      would anger shareholders. And limiting pay could send bankers fleeing to \n      hedge funds, where there are fewer rules.<br>\n      <br>\n      And that’s the VOA Special English Economics Report, written by Mario \n      Ritter. These groups are the American Society for \n      Microbiology and the American Cleaning Institute, formerly the Soap and \n      Detergent Association.<br>\n      <br>\n      There was good news in the latest study. That was the most yet since the studies began in nineteen \n      ninety-six.<br>\n      <br>\n      Researchers visited restrooms at a baseball park in Atlanta and a science \n      museum and aquarium in Chicago. Atlanta followed \n      at eighty-two percent. Ninety-six percent \n      of them said they always wash their hands after using public restrooms.<br>\n      <br>\n      Almost nine out of ten also said they always wash after using the bathroom \n      at home.<br>\n      <br>\n      Hand washing can help prevent the spread of many different infections.<br>\n      <br>\n      To clean your hands well, wet them first and rub in soap for at least \n      twenty seconds, including between the fingers and under the nails.<br>\n      <br>\n      Then rinse under running water. In a public restroom, if you dry your \n      hands with a paper towel, you should also use the towel to shut off the \n      water and open the door.<br>\n      <br>\n      Hand washing is also important when preparing food and after changing a \n      baby\'s diaper. You should also wash if you cough or sneeze into your \n      hands.<br>\n      <br>\n      If you use an alcohol-based product instead of soap and water, make sure \n      it contains at least sixty percent alcohol.<br>\n      <br>\n      And that’s the VOA Special English Health Report, written by Caty Weaver. \n      I’m Bob Doughty.<p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      　<p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      VOA<span lang=\"zh-cn\">咕噜英语学习网</span>&nbsp;\n      www.guluwang.com <span lang=\"zh-cn\">\n      www.guluwang.com(咕噜网)</span></td>\n    \n  \n  ','http://voanews.cn/SpecialEnglish/2010/October/mp3/spec0041a0929.mp3');

A planetarium is a theater with a rounded ceiling onto which \n      images of the stars and planets are projected. Planetariums give \n      educational shows about astronomy and what you can see in the night sky. \n      Today, we tell about the past, present, and future of planetariums. And, \n      we visit the Albert Einstein Planetarium at the Smithsonian National Air \n      and Space Museum in Washington, D.C.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: Since ancient times, humans have worked on ways to understand \n      and represent the movement of the stars and planets. Experts credit the \n      Greek astronomer Archimedes with developing the earliest known device to \n      show the daily movement of the planets. He lived more than two thousand \n      years ago. These mechanical devices that show the relative placement and \n      movement of the planets and moons are sometimes called orreries. Over the \n      centuries, scientific thinkers worked to develop these devices and improve \n      their accuracy and complexity.<br>\n      <br>\n      The words “orrery” and “planetarium” were once used interchangeably. \n      Today, the world “planetarium” generally means a theater inside a dome.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021172232.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"120\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          The \n          original Carl Zeiss projector at the Smithsonian\'s Albert Einstein \n          Planetarium projects an image from the show &quot;Infinity Express: A \n          20-Minute Tour of the Universe&quot;</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      BARBARA KLEIN: The earliest \n      planetarium that is still working today is in the Netherlands, although \n      the device is actually an orrery. It was built by a man named Eise Eisinga \n      starting in seventeen seventy-four. It took him seven years to build this \n      moving device inside a room in his house. All the planets move at the same \n      speed as the real planets in our solar system. So, it takes one year for \n      Earth to move around the sun and about twenty-nine years for Saturn to do \n      so. Eisinga made his device out of wood, metal nails, a clock and nine \n      weights.<br>\n      <br>\n      Other versions of early planetariums were large globes. People could sit \n      inside them. Holes were cut into the walls of these globes to represent \n      stars.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: A group of German engineers and scientists helped develop the \n      modern planetarium between nineteen ten and nineteen thirty. The creators \n      of the Deutsches Museum of science and technology in Munich wanted to \n      build a planetarium. So, they asked the Carl Zeiss company in Germany to \n      help with this plan. This company was known for making scientific \n      equipment such as microscopes.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021172233.jpg\" width=\"120\" height=\"171\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          The \n          oldest working planetarium was built by Eise Eisinga in the Dutch town \n          of Frankston</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      It took engineers at Zeiss \n      several years to invent a new planetarium technology. The complex \n      mechanical device they made projected light through “star plates” of film \n      that contained images of thousands of stars. Public viewings of the first \n      Zeiss planetarium projector began in nineteen twenty-three.<br>\n      <br>\n      BARBARA KLEIN: Soon, other cities in Europe and later in the United States \n      began ordering planetarium devices from Zeiss. An American business leader \n      named Max Adler learned about these planetariums and traveled to Germany \n      to see one for himself.<br>\n      <br>\n      He was so amazed with the Zeiss device that he donated the money for a \n      planetarium to be built in his native Chicago, Illinois.<br>\n      <br>\n      The Adler Planetarium was the first modern planetarium in the United \n      States. It opened its doors to the public in nineteen thirty. Planetariums \n      soon opened in Philadelphia, Pennsylvania; Los Angeles, California and New \n      York City.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: Planetarium technology continues to evolve and improve to \n      this day. But what if you live far away from a city with a planetarium?<br>\n      <br>\n      Dan Neafus helps supervise the Gates Planetarium at the Denver Museum of \n      Nature and Science in Colorado. He says new technologies are helping to \n      connect planetariums with viewers in other areas. There are relatively few \n      digital planetariums around the world. But this technology could bring \n      space education and the experience of a planetarium show to more people \n      through the use of a computer and Internet connection.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021172234.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          \n          Planetarium in Chicago was the first modern planetarium in the United \n          States</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      BARBARA KLEIN: The Loch Ness \n      Production company in Colorado makes shows, images and music for the \n      planetarium community. The company also gathers facts about planetariums \n      around the world. It says there are over one thousand five hundred \n      planetariums in the United States. Many kinds of organizations have \n      planetariums. They include museums, science centers, universities, schools \n      and even astronomy clubs. Some planetariums have domes that measure over \n      twenty meters, while others are much smaller.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: Some planetariums use film projections. More technologically \n      advanced planetariums use digital systems controlled by computers. Digital \n      technologies offer planetariums many choices. These theaters can show \n      movies about space. Or, these planetariums can serve as classrooms where \n      live interactive presentations take place. They are extremely massive \n      concentrations of matter. The actor Liam Neeson narrates the movie.<br>\n      <br>\n      LIAM NEESON: “How do you find something that hides in the dark? You have \n      to look for its tell-tale signs. Swift’s instruments are designed to \n      record bursts of high energy radiation. Gamma rays don’t penetrate Earth’s \n      atmosphere, but out here in space, Swift’s view of them is front row \n      center. They erupt when a black hole is born. That happens when a large \n      star dies in a blaze of glory called a super nova.”<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: It takes a lot of work to produce a good planetarium movie. \n      Experts at the Denver Museum of Nature and Science made the “Black Holes” \n      movie. They worked with movie producers, computer experts, astronomers, \n      astrophysicists and other professionals. Their film is scientifically \n      correct and also a lot of fun to watch. Many images in the movie are based \n      on complex mathematical calculations about space gathered by scientists.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      BARBARA KLEIN: Next week, the ‘Imiloa Astronomy Center in Hawaii will host \n      a six-day ‘Imiloa Fulldome Film Festival. Museum and planetarium \n      professionals from around the world will be able to watch some of the \n      latest movies available for digital planetarium theaters.<br>\n      <br>\n      The ‘Imiloa Astronomy Center is part of the University of Hawaii at Hilo. \n      The center’s planetarium has the world’s first three-dimensional \n      planetarium system. Viewers wear special glasses to experience this \n      effect. The Astronomy Center is an example of how technologies will \n      continue to change and improve experiencing the night sky in planetariums \n      of the future.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: This program was written and produced by Dana Demange. INSERT INTO `dede_addonmusic` VALUES('413','58','','','119.6.30.75','','','\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">　\n</td>\n        \nSHIRLEY GRIFFITH: Welcome to \n      the MAKING OF A NATION -- American history in VOA Special English.<br>\n      <br>\n      A new leader stood before the American people on March fourth, nineteen \n      thirteen. He was Woodrow Wilson -- the twenty-eighth president of the \n      United States.<br>\n      <br>\n      Wilson belonged to the Democratic Party. He was progressive in his belief \n      that government should take an active part in efforts for social reforms.<br>\n      <br>\n      This week in our series, Kay Gallant and Harry Monroe begin the story of \n      Wilson\'s presidency.<br>\n      <br>\n      KAY GALANT: Woodrow Wilson had spent most of his life at Princeton \n      University. First he was a professor. Then he was university president. \n      Next, Wilson was elected governor of the state of New Jersey. His early \n      success as governor made him a leading candidate for the Democratic \n      presidential nomination in nineteen twelve.<br>\n      <br>\n      Wilson traveled widely around the country during the campaign. He made \n      speeches to many groups. He tried to make himself and his ideas known to \n      as many Americans as possible.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021172027.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"113\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          Woodrow \n          Wilson and his cabinet seated around table, 1913</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      HARRY MONROE: Wilson called \n      his program &quot;the New Freedom.&quot; One of his campaign promises was to fight \n      for better conditions for America\'s small business owners.<br>\n      <br>\n      Such proposals helped him win the Democratic nomination for president. \n      Then he defeated President William Howard Taft and former president \n      Theodore Roosevelt in the election. Woodrow Wilson, the former president \n      of a university, had become the president of a nation.<br>\n      <br>\n      The largest crowd in Washington, D.C.\'s history welcomed Wilson outside \n      the Capitol Building on the day of his inauguration. He called on the \n      American people to join him in making the country a better place. &quot;Our \n      duty,&quot; Wilson said, &quot;is to correct the evil without hurting the good. I \n      call all honest men, all patriotic, all forward-looking men to my side.&quot;<br>\n      <br>\n      KAY GALANT: Wilson wasted no time. He immediately called a special session \n      of Congress to act on Democratic campaign promises to reduce import taxes, \n      or tariffs. Wilson felt strongly about the need to reform these taxes. He \n      broke tradition by leaving the White House to appear before Congress, in \n      person, to appeal for his tariff proposals.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021172028.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          A crowd \n          gathering for the Woodrow Wilson\'s inauguration parade in 1913</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      Many members of Congress \n      opposed Wilson\'s plans. But the new president used the results of a Senate \n      investigation to win the fight. The investigation showed that a number of \n      senators owned companies that depended on high tariffs for their profits. \n      The votes of these senators were influenced by their property holdings.<br>\n      <br>\n      Public knowledge of the situation forced many of them to give up their \n      holdings and stop resisting tariff reform. Congress finally approved \n      Wilson\'s proposals.<br>\n      <br>\n      HARRY MONROE: Lower tariffs reduced the amount of money taken in by the \n      federal government. So the Senate also approved a tax on income, or \n      earnings. A constitutional amendment had been passed earlier to permit \n      such a tax.<br>\n      <br>\n      President Wilson and the Democratic Party were pleased with the new tariff \n      and income tax bills. But they were far from finished. Next they turned \n      their efforts to reform of the banking industry.<br>\n      <br>\n      For several years, many people had recognized the need for changes in the \n      banking system. The old system of uncontrolled private banks had developed \n      years earlier, before the United States became a major industrial nation. \n      Many people agreed that a more modern system was needed. But they could \n      not agree on details.<br>\n      <br>\n      KAY GALANT: President Wilson said control of the nation\'s wealth was held \n      by too few men. He noted a report that said just two men controlled ten \n      percent of the total wealth of the United States.<br>\n      <br>\n      Wilson said the nation needed a money supply that could be increased or \n      reduced, when necessary, to correct economic conditions. He said a method \n      was needed to let banks help each other during economic emergencies. And \n      he said laws were needed to prevent a few wealthy men from using the \n      economic resources of the country for their own purposes.<br>\n      <br>\n      Finally, Wilson said, &quot;The control of this system of banking must be \n      public, not private. It must belong to the government itself.&quot;<br>\n      <br>\n      HARRY MONROE: Wilson called his proposal for a central bank the Federal \n      Reserve System.<br>\n      <br>\n      Under the plan, the nation would be divided into twelve areas. Each area \n      would have its own federal reserve bank. These area banks would not do \n      business with the public. They would serve only as &quot;bankers\' banks.&quot; And \n      they would issue a new form of money supported by the federal government. \n      Most important, the leaders of the new system would be chosen by the \n      government--not by private business.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021172029.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          Woodrow \n          Wilson</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      KAY GALANT: Bankers, business \n      leaders, and their representatives in Congress sharply criticized \n      President Wilson\'s proposals. They said government control of the banking \n      system was socialism, not capitalism.<br>\n      <br>\n      But Wilson refused to change his proposals. And he helped to lead the \n      fight to make them law. Finally, Congress agreed.<br>\n      <br>\n      It did not take long for bankers to discover that the new system was much \n      better than the old one. Today, the Federal Reserve System is one of the \n      most important institutions in the United States.<br>\n      <br>\n      HARRY MONROE: For Woodrow Wilson, the fight over the banking system was \n      yet another political success. He had won major reforms in the nation\'s \n      tariffs, taxes, and banking systems. Now he told Congress that new \n      legislation was needed to control the power of monopolies and trusts. \n      These were the giant companies and business alliances that controlled \n      complete industries.<br>\n      <br>\n      Wilson proposed a new anti-trust law to control the actions of large \n      companies. His supporters in Congress wrote a bill that listed a number of \n      business activities that no longer would be permitted.<br>\n      <br>\n      For example, no longer could a company set prices that would reduce \n      competition or create a monopoly. No longer could corporations buy stocks \n      of competing companies. No longer could they demand that a store refuse to \n      sell competing products. The new bill also protected labor unions from \n      being charged with anti-trust violations. It gave unions more power to \n      organize and protect workers.<br>\n      <br>\n      KAY GALANT: At President Wilson\'s request, Congress also prepared a law \n      that set up a government agency called the Federal Trade Commission. The \n      commission was given the job of investigating wrong-doing in business. It \n      had the power to force companies to obey the new anti-trust laws and other \n      rules.<br>\n      <br>\n      Both the anti-trust law and the Federal Trade Commission helped protect \n      small business owners from the power of business giants. Once again, the \n      proposals caused fierce debate. But, once again, Congress finally voted to \n      give Wilson most of what he wanted.<br>\n      <br>\n      HARRY MONROE: The early months of Wilson\'s term were one of the most \n      successful times in the history of any president. The new president had \n      won the election by promising major reforms in the economic life of the \n      country. And he had kept that promise.<br>\n      <br>\n      The reforms were not only a victory for Woodrow Wilson. They also changed \n      the face of American business and economics for many years to come. The \n      income tax, for example, grew to become the federal government\'s main \n      source of money.<br>\n      <br>\n      KAY GALANT: Woodrow Wilson had taught history in the days when he was a \n      professor at Princeton University. He knew his actions as president could \n      influence the country for a long time. But, as a historian, he also knew \n      his own term in the White House could be changed by unexpected events. \n      That is just what happened.<br>\n      <br>\n      Wilson campaigned for president mainly on national issues. But he soon was \n      forced to spend more and more time on international issues. His first big \n      problem was across the United States\' southern border, in Mexico. That \n      will be our story next week.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: Our program was written by Frank Beardsley. His father died when Roger was \n      only one year old. His mother became sick soon after. Roger was sent to \n      live with his uncle in Erick, Oklahoma.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021171818.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"113\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          Singer \n          and songwriter Roger Miller arrives at the American Music Awards in \n          Los Angeles in 1987</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      Roger Miller had a difficult \n      childhood. Most of his days were spent working on his uncle’s farm, \n      picking cotton. He was a lonely and unhappy child. Roger began writing \n      songs because he loved the music he heard on the radio. He also learned to \n      play the guitar and the violin, sometimes called a fiddle. Much later, he \n      learned to play the drums. Music helped the young Roger escape the hard \n      work on his uncle’s cotton farm.<br>\n      <br>\n      SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: Roger left the farm when he was still very young. INSERT INTO `dede_addonmusic` VALUES('408','58','','','119.6.30.75','','','\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">　\n</td>\n        \nBARBARA KLEIN: I’m Barbara \n      Klein.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: And I\'m Steve Ember with PEOPLE IN AMERICA in VOA Special \n      English. Today we tell about writer Ralph Ellison and his famous novel \n      “Invisible Man.” The book is about a nameless black man’s search for his \n      identity and place in society.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      BARBARA KLEIN: Ralph Ellison\'s novel, “Invisible Man”, was published in \n      nineteen fifty-two. Ellison was at once called a major new writer. The \n      book won the National Book Award, a high and rare honor for a first novel.<br>\n      <br>\n      Since then millions of copies have been printed. The book is still used in \n      many universities and other schools. One professor said that he has used \n      the book in his teaching for twenty-five years. He said that each time he \n      returns to “Invisible Man” he finds new ideas in it. Ellison writes in the \n      beginning of his book:<br>\n      <br>\n      READER: &quot;I am an invisible man … I am a man of substance, flesh and bone, \n      fiber and liquids C and I might even be said to possess a mind. I am \n      invisible, understand, simply because people refuse to see me…When they \n      approach me they see only my surroundings, themselves, or figments of \n      their imagination C indeed, everything and anything except me.”<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021171514.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"113\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          Ralph \n          Ellison, New York author, is a witness, August 30, 1966, at a Senate \n          Subcommittee hearing in Washington during continuing hearings on the \n          racial problems in big cities.</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      STEVE EMBER: From the start, \n      “Invisible Man” was a book that changed the way white Americans thought \n      about black Americans. It also changed the way black Americans thought \n      about themselves. And it caused major disputes among both black and white \n      critics.<br>\n      <br>\n      Black critics said the book was too difficult to read. One black critic \n      said that the black man needed “Invisible Man” like he needed a knife in \n      his back. Another black writer dismissed Ellison because Ellison demanded \n      that writing skills must be learned before political ideas can be \n      expressed.<br>\n      <br>\n      Some white critics refused to accept a black writer who did not write from \n      direct anger at whites. They seemed to want him not to write from his \n      mind, but from the color of his skin. Yet the book continues to live long \n      after most people have forgotten the disputes.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021171515.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          Ralph \n          Ellison was one of six finalists for an award from the National Book \n          Foundation for the best fiction winner in the foundation\'s awards \n          60-year history. (1964)</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      BARBARA KLEIN: Ralph Ellison \n      was born in nineteen fourteen, in Oklahoma City, Oklahoma. His father died \n      when Ralph was three. His mother supported herself and her son by cleaning \n      other people\'s houses.<br>\n      <br>\n      She also supported her son\'s interest in music and writing. She would take \n      home old music recordings and magazines from the houses where she worked. \n      Ralph liked jazz, and played trumpet in his high school band. He dreamed \n      of writing serious music.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: In nineteen thirty-three, Ralph entered a black university, \n      Tuskegee Institute, in the state of Alabama. He wanted to study music. He \n      moved to New York City in nineteen thirty-six. He still planned to study \n      music and art. However, that same year he ran out of money and could no \n      longer attend school.<br>\n      <br>\n      The nineteen thirties in America were difficult economic times. There were \n      not many jobs to be found, and even fewer for black men. Ellison worked at \n      many things. He shined people\'s shoes. He played trumpet in a jazz band. \n      He worked for the Young Men\'s Christian Association. He worked in \n      factories. He worked for a brief time taking pictures. Lack of money was \n      an important reason for Ralph Ellison becoming a writer. He said:<br>\n      <br>\n      READER: &quot;I have always read a lot, and I began to realize I had a certain \n      talent for it. It was not easy to be the kind of musician I wanted to be: \n      I did not have enough money to go to Juilliard [school of music]. So I \n      stuck with what I had.”<br>\n      <br>\n      BARBARA KLEIN: In New York City, Ellison joined the Federal Writers \n      Project. This was a program created during Franklin Roosevelt\'s presidency \n      to keep writers employed at writing.<br>\n      <br>\n      He met two important black writers, Langston Hughes and Richard Wright. \n      Wright soon would publish “Native Son,” the book that made him famous.<br>\n      <br>\n      Later, during World War Two, Ellison served as a cook in the United States \n      Merchant Marine. Merchant marine ships carried war supplies to American \n      and allied soldiers. For Ellison, the war was a time of learning and \n      trying to write.<br>\n      <br>\n      He read books by the American writers T.S. Eliot, Ernest Hemingway, and \n      William Faulkner. And he read books by foreign writers like the Irish \n      writer James Joyce.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: Ralph Ellison\'s stories were first published during World War \n      Two. When the war was over, he visited a friend in the state of Vermont. \n      Ellison said:<br>\n      <br>\n      READER: &quot;One day I wrote, \'I am an invisible man.’ I did not know what \n      those words represented at the start, and I had no thought about what gave \n      me the idea.&quot;<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101021171516.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          &quot;The \n          Invisible Man&quot; sculpture stands in front of the longtime home of \n          acclaimed author Ralph Ellison in New York\'s West Harlem.</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      STEVE EMBER: The book that \n      started with those words took almost seven years to write.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      BARBARA KLEIN: Like many other novels, Ellison\'s story is a series of \n      experiences as the storyteller learns to deal with life. Yet, unlike other \n      novels, “Invisible Man” takes place in a dream-like atmosphere in the \n      United States. It is a world where dreams come close to reality, and the \n      real world looks like a frightening dream.<br>\n      <br>\n      The man telling his story in “Invisible Man” lives in a hidden underground \n      space. But to prove that he exists, at least to himself, he has lit his \n      underground room with one thousand three hundred sixty-nine lights. They \n      remain lit with power he has stolen from the electric company.<br>\n      <br>\n      In much of Ellison\'s novel the person telling the story is a victim, \n      usually of white people, but also of some blacks. He both loves and hates \n      the world. He plans some day to leave his underground shelter. He says \n      that as a man he is willing to believe that &quot;even the invisible victim is \n      responsible for the fate of all.”<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: The man telling the story says that as a boy, white men \n      covered his eyes with a cloth. The white men tell the boy to blindly fight \n      other black boys. The blacks are forced to fight each other to please \n      whites.<br>\n      <br>\n      At the end of the novel the story has moved from the American South to the \n      North. There are riots in Harlem, the black area of New York City. Instead \n      of ten black children fighting each other blindly, grown black men are \n      battling each other to the death. Blacks still are having their strength \n      turned upon themselves.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      BARBARA KLEIN: Critics said “Invisible Man” was well written. But some \n      critics called this a weakness. They said the writing seemed to hide the \n      book\'s ideas and make them less a product of black life.<br>\n      <br>\n      One critic said that he found it difficult to call “Invisible Man” an \n      African-American novel. He said that the main person in the book is a \n      southern black man. But, the critic said, he is all of us, no matter where \n      we were born or the color of our skin.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: After “Invisible Man” was published in nineteen fifty-two, \n      Ralph Ellison taught at a number of universities. He retired from New York \n      University in nineteen eighty. While he was alive, he published only two \n      other books. They were books of criticism and essays, called “Shadow and \n      Act” and “Going to the Territory.”<br>\n      <br>\n      Ralph Ellison died in nineteen ninety-four, at the age of eighty. After \n      his death, a book of his stories, “Flying Home,” was published. Shortly \n      before his death, Ellison had told someone that his second novel was \n      almost finished. He had worked on the novel for forty years without \n      finishing it.<br>\n      <br>\n      Parts of the book had appeared in magazines during the nineteen sixties \n      and seventies. Ellison had to rewrite the novel after a large part of it \n      was burned in a fire at his home in nineteen sixty-seven. The novel was \n      said to be two thousand pages long. Finally, his friend John Callahan put \n      the book together after Ellison died. The novel was published in nineteen \n      ninety-nine. It was called “Juneteenth.”<br>\n      <br>\n      BARBARA KLEIN: Since “Invisible Man” was published, many American writers \n      have said how much Ellison influenced them.<br>\n      <br>\n      In nineteen ninety, another black writer, Charles Johnson, was given the \n      National Book Award. In receiving the prize, Johnson thanked Ralph Ellison \n      for leading the way for black writers. Ellison was present at the \n      ceremony. He thanked Johnson. Then he expressed his belief that black \n      writers should not be influenced only by other black writers. He said:<br>\n      <br>\n      READER: &quot;You do not write out of your skin. You write out of your ideas \n      and the quality of your mind.&quot;<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: This program was written by Richard Thorman and produced by \n      Lawan Davis. It is the only city in \n      the United States that can claim to have defended itself from American \n      Indians, fierce pirates, Spanish ships, French soldiers, and British \n      forces. It was first in many things. Charleston had the first continual \n      train service in the United States.<br>\n      <br>\n      It built the first museum and the first public flower garden in America. \n      And the first battle of the American Civil War took place on a very small \n      but important island in its port.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/201010102117122.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"113\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          Tourists \n          on a carriage tour in Charleston in February. South Carolina tourism \n          officials say tourism has grown to an $18.4 billion industry in the \n          state.</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      Charleston has some of the \n      most beautiful and unusual homes in America. One critic has called \n      Charleston the most friendly city in the United States. Charleston is all \n      of these things and much more.<br>\n      <br>\n      SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: Plan your visit to Charleston for early spring, late \n      autumn or the winter months. The citizens of Charleston will tell you \n      their lovely city is not fun in the summer. It is extremely hot. The \n      summer heat is important to the history of Charleston.<br>\n      <br>\n      Early settlers owned huge farms called plantations. In the seventeen \n      hundreds, these farms produced a plant called indigo which is used to make \n      cloth the color blue.<br>\n      <br>\n      Many plantation owners forced slaves to do the work needed to grow indigo \n      in the extreme heat. Slavery became important to the economy of \n      Charleston. The plantations, indigo and slavery are part of the history of \n      the city.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: At least three Indian tribes were living in the area that \n      became Charleston when Spanish explorers arrived in Fifteen-Twenty-One. \n      The Spanish explorers, and later, French explorers tried to establish \n      settlements near that area but none lasted.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/201010102117123.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          Shoppers \n          at the City Market in Charleston</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      English settlers first came \n      to the area in sixteen seventy. They established a town. They called it \n      Charles Town in honor of the English King, Charles the Second. The people \n      of the city changed its name to Charleston in seventeen eighty-three.<br>\n      <br>\n      SHIRLEY GRIFFITH:<br>\n      <br>\n      Many people came to live in Charleston because it produced indigo and had \n      a good port. The people who settled the area were hard working and \n      independent. They considered themselves citizens of England.<br>\n      <br>\n      Still, they did not like some of the laws declared by the English \n      government. The colonists successfully defended their city many times in \n      the early seventeen hundreds. They defended it against both French and \n      Spanish forces, and against raids by Yamasee Indians and by pirates.<br>\n      <br>\n      In seventeen nineteen, the citizens of Charleston rebelled against the \n      group of English men who controlled their colony. They wanted more \n      self-government. Britain\'s King George agreed. This change gave the people \n      of Charleston a feeling of independence.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: Charleston is still proud of its part in the war for \n      independence. The city provided several political and military leaders \n      during the American Revolution.<br>\n      <br>\n      British forces attacked it two times, but were defeated by the people of \n      Charleston. The third time, the British captured the city and held it for \n      more than a year.<br>\n      <br>\n      Charleston continued to grow after the American colonists had won their \n      independence from England. The new federal government knew that the city \n      was important.<br>\n      <br>\n      Workers began building a strong base to guard Charleston in eighteen \n      twenty-eight. This base was on a small island in Charleston Harbor. It was \n      named Fort Sumter. It was designed to guard the city from any future \n      enemy.<br>\n      <br>\n      SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: There were no thoughts of war or future enemies while \n      Fort Sumter was being built. The plantations near Charleston had began to \n      plant new crops like rice and cotton.<br>\n      <br>\n      With the help of slave labor, cotton became extremely important to the \n      economy of Charleston and much of the South. Many people in the northern \n      United States began to think that slavery was very wrong, however.<br>\n      <br>\n      Slave owners in the South wanted things to remain as they had always been. \n      They believed the federal government had no right to tell them what they \n      could or could not do.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: A national crisis began when Abraham Lincoln was elected \n      president in eighteen sixty. The people of South Carolina believed he \n      would try to end slavery by force. They voted to leave the United States. \n      They were quickly followed by other southern states. These southern states \n      soon created the government of the Confederate States of America.<br>\n      <br>\n      Federal troops controlled Fort Sumter when South Carolina voted to leave \n      the Union. The people of Charleston demanded the federal troops leave. The \n      Union commander refused.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/201010102117124.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          On April \n          12, 1861, a cannon fired at Fort Sumter was the first shot of the \n          Civil War</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      On the morning of April \n      twelfth, eighteen sixty-one, a cannon was fired at Fort Sumter. It was the \n      first shot of America\'s long Civil War.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: Charleston suffered a lot of damage during the Civil \n      War. Several major battles were fought there. Late in the war another \n      battle for control of Fort Sumter continued for almost two years. Much of \n      Charleston had been destroyed by the time the war ended. Rebuilding the \n      city was a long and slow process.<br>\n      <br>\n      The people of Charleston tried to save the historic buildings from the \n      seventeen hundreds. They wanted to keep those buildings they felt were an \n      important part of their city.<br>\n      <br>\n      The huge plantations near Charleston were also in need of rebuilding. Many \n      owners failed in their efforts because they could no longer use slave \n      labor. Their farms became much smaller.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: The historic buildings of Charleston were affected by weather \n      as well as wars. Through the years, ocean storms have severely damaged the \n      city. A major storm struck Charleston in September nineteen-eighty-nine. \n      It killed eighteen people and caused more than three-thousand-million \n      dollars in damage.<br>\n      <br>\n      The huge storm had winds of more than two hundred seventeen kilometers an \n      hour. It caused high waves that severely flooded city streets.<br>\n      <br>\n      SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: The federal, state and city governments and individual \n      citizens have spent millions of dollars to rebuild and repair historic \n      areas. So in some places, Charleston looks a lot like it has for several \n      hundred years.<br>\n      <br>\n      In the center of the city are stores in small one-hundred year old \n      buildings. The same family has owned one of the stores for almost one \n      hundred fifty years. Fine eating places throughout the city serve southern \n      food.<br>\n      <br>\n      The people of Charleston will tell you they have some of the best eating \n      places in the United States. Many visitors agree.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: Beautiful, old buildings are a major reason thousands of \n      people visit Charleston each year. One of the famous buildings is the Old \n      Exchange and Provost Dungeon. It was built in the early seventeen \n      hundreds. It was a jail that held the famous English pirate Stede Bonnet \n      and his crew before they were hanged.<br>\n      <br>\n      Several of the old plantation farms near Charleston also are open to \n      visitors. One is called Boone Hall Plantation. It is still a working farm. \n      Boone Hall Plantation looks much like it did before the American Civil \n      War. It has been used as the setting for a number of movies and television \n      programs about the American South and the Civil War.<br>\n      <br>\n      SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: From almost anywhere along the waterfront in Charleston, \n      you can see a large American flag flying over the small island that still \n      holds Fort Sumter. Most visitors go to the historic fort during their time \n      in Charleston. Several companies provide boat rides to the fort.<br>\n      <br>\n      Much of the fort was destroyed during the Civil War. But what remains of \n      Fort Sumter is protected by the National Park Service. Park workers meet \n      each boat and explain about the battles that took place.<br>\n      <br>\n      STEVE EMBER: Charleston has many interesting places to visit. However the \n      people who live in the city really make it special. They are extremely \n      friendly in a way that is part of the culture of the American south. The \n      people of Charleston continue to keep their city beautiful using modern \n      technology to protect their historic past.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      Our program was written by Paul Thompson and produced by Lawan Davis. \n      Internet users can read and listen to our reports at voaspecialenglish.com. \n      With Shirley Griffith, I\'m Steve Ember. About 260 of those are feature films, 122 of them  are world premieres and they are from over 60 countries.&quot; Bailey heads a  team of 20 programmers who scour the world every year for new films  from veteran directors and first-timers. Bailey says they stress the  &quot;international&quot; part of the festival title and it is something Canadian  audiences welcome:<br />\r\n<br />\r\n&quot;I think we pride ourselves on being open-minded and curious about the  rest of the world. We are not as inward looking as, maybe, some other  countries &hellip;partly because, I think, particularly Toronto is such a  diverse city. The majority of the population is people who were not born  in Toronto, so we are outward looking. We are curious to see what the  world is bringing to us and I think we pride ourselves on being engaged  with the world, being fair minded, having a kind of ironic sense of  humor about things (and) certainly never taking ourselves too seriously.  I think you\'ll find that in the audiences at the movies here at the  Festival,&quot; he said.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nIrish writer-director Juanita Wilson was particularly moved by the  audience response to her film &quot;As If I Am Not There,&quot; a heartbreaking  drama about women forced to be sex slaves during the war in Bosnia.<br />\r\n<br />\r\n&quot;There was a Bosnian couple who came up to talk to me. They had left  because of the war and the woman was saying that she couldn\'t even  listen to music from home because it was so sad and so upsetting. You actually make  it so that you can touch the hearts of real people and when you do it is  such a privilege that you can do that when people are open to it. If they are watching the audience and the audience  is into it and the filmmaker is pleased by that and this is an  experience that they haven\'t had yet - this is the world premiere of  their film and they\'re loving it and if they\'ll come back as a result  with their next film, then that\'s success for me,&quot; he said.<br />\r\n<br />\r\nCameron Bailey is co-director of the 35th annual Toronto International Film Festival.</span></p>','http://down02.putclub.com/voanews/audio/2010/09/19/silverman_toronto_film_festival_feature_cut_17sep10-32b.mp3');

Some see money in these little bloodsuckers.<br>\n      <br>\n      Missy Henriksen speaks for the National Pest Management Association.<br>\n      <br>\n      MISSY HENRIKSEN: &quot;We are now seeing bedbugs in what we would consider to \n      be atypical locations: schools and hospitals, retail locations, movie \n      theaters. So, as the numbers have grown, those bed bugs are spreading out \n      and traveling along with people.&quot;<br>\n      <br>\n      New York and other cities have outbreaks. But the United States is not the \n      only country affected.<br>\n      <br>\n      Jeff White is an insect expert who hosts Bed Bug TV on the website BedBug \n      Central.<br>\n      <br>\n      JEFF WHITE: &quot;What has caused this rapid expansion of bedbug infestations \n      -- and the world for that matter -- is the lack of public awareness.&quot;<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101013532747.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"113\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          \n          Entomologist Jeff White holds a container of live bed bugs at the \n          North American Bed Bug Summit in Rosemont, Illinois, on September 21</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      Mr. White says bedbugs nearly \n      disappeared from the United States for fifty or sixty years. Now \n      researchers are looking for faster, safer ways to control them without the \n      kinds of poisons used in the past.<br>\n      <br>\n      The name is misleading. Bedbugs do not just live in beds. Mr. White says \n      they can survive for a year without food -- that is, blood.<br>\n      <br>\n      Last week, an industry event called BedBug University\'s North American \n      Summit 2010 took place near Chicago, Illinois. More than three hundred \n      sixty people attended the two-day meeting.<br>\n      <br>\n      The industry says bedbugs are the most difficult pest to control. \n      Treatments can cost from several hundred dollars to thousands of dollars \n      in, say, a hotel or apartment building.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101013532748.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          A common \n          bedbug full of blood after feeding on a human arm</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      Missy Henriksen says \n      Americans spent almost two hundred sixty million dollars on bedbug \n      treatments last year. That was only five percent of total spending on pest \n      control, she says, but that number does not include other costs.<br>\n      <br>\n      MISSY HENRIKSEN: &quot;I think the overall economic impact because of bedbugs \n      is certainly much more significant. We\'re finding that businesses who have \n      bedbugs oftentimes will close to remediate the problem.&quot;<br>\n      <br>\n      Bedbugs have not been shown to spread disease. But they can leave itchy \n      bite marks and cause allergic reactions in some people.<br>\n      <br>\n      Lately, however, another bug has caught America\'s attention. Wilson took office in \n      nineteen thirteen. He moved quickly to fulfill his campaign promises. He \n      won congressional approval for lower import taxes, a new tax on earnings, \n      and restrictions on the power of big companies.<br>\n      <br>\n      These were some of the most important economic reforms the nation had seen \n      in many years.<br>\n      <br>\n      This week in our series, Larry West and Maurice Joyce continue the story \n      of Wilson\'s administration.<br>\n      <br>\n      LARRY WEST: Most of Woodrow Wilson\'s political victories were on national \n      issues. He had little experience with international issues. But foreign \n      events soon began to demand more and more of his time. With all of his \n      successes at home, it is a surprising fact of history that his presidency \n      is remembered best for its foreign policy.<br>\n      <br>\n      The story of Woodrow Wilson\'s foreign policy is full of high ideas and \n      political bravery. But it also is a story of fierce struggle and lost \n      hopes. It is a story that begins across America\'s southern border--in \n      Mexico.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101013532955.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"113\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          Woodrow \n          Wilson and his cabinet seated around table, 1913</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      MAURICE JOYCE: At that time, \n      Mexico had been ruled for many years by Porfirio Diaz. As Diaz grew older, \n      his power began to weaken. In nineteen eleven, a revolt broke out. It was \n      led by Francisco Madero, the leader of a land reform movement. Diaz \n      understood he could not win. He resigned and fled the country.<br>\n      <br>\n      Madero declared himself president. However, powerful groups in Mexico \n      opposed him. In a short time, one of his own generals, Victoriano Huerta, \n      arrested him. Madero was murdered soon after Huerta seized power.<br>\n      <br>\n      President Wilson refused to recognize Huerta\'s government. He believed \n      other forces would rise up against him. Wilson was right. Another revolt \n      began, led by General Venustiano Carranza.<br>\n      <br>\n      LARRY WEST: Wilson offered aid to Carranza. Carranza rejected the offer. \n      He was afraid of American interference in Mexico. He told Wilson that \n      Mexican troops would do all the fighting. He only wanted guns and \n      ammunition.<br>\n      <br>\n      American forces did, however, get involved in the conflict. President \n      Wilson learned that a ship from Germany was bringing supplies to the \n      Huerta government. The ship would land at the Mexican port of Vera Cruz. \n      Wilson ordered the United States Navy to seize and occupy the port. The \n      move started a storm of criticism in the United States and throughout \n      Latin America.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      MAURICE JOYCE: Many people denounced President Wilson. They called him an \n      imperialist and a fool. They asked: what right did the United States have \n      to interfere in Mexico. Wilson finally stopped American military action in \n      Mexico. He tried to settle the dispute at an international conference at \n      Niagara Falls, Canada. The effort failed. The conference did not produce a \n      settlement.<br>\n      <br>\n      While the diplomats were talking, Carranza\'s revolutionary forces were \n      fighting. They moved on Mexico City, the capital. President Huerta fled. \n      Carranza formed a new government.<br>\n      <br>\n      LARRY WEST: The new government began to split apart almost immediately. \n      Another general, Francisco &quot;Pancho&quot; Villa, tried to seize power. He forced \n      Carranza out of Mexico City. Then he formed his own government. President \n      Wilson recognized Villa and his government.<br>\n      <br>\n      Carranza, however, refused to give up. Day by day, his army grew stronger. \n      He forced Villa to retreat. Then President Wilson recognized Carranza\'s \n      government. Like Carranza, Villa refused to give up. He decided to try to \n      start a war between Mexico and the United States.<br>\n      <br>\n      Pancho Villa wanted the United States to attack Carranza. Then he would \n      step in to lead Mexican forces in battle. That would make him a hero. With \n      this plan in mind, Pancho Villa attacked an American town across the \n      border in Texas. He killed nineteen persons.<br>\n      <br>\n      MAURICE JOYCE: President Wilson immediately ordered a large American force \n      to find and punish Villa. At first, Carranza welcomed the move. Villa was \n      his enemy. He wanted him captured. Then Carranza began to fear that the \n      American troops might threaten his government. He demanded the withdrawal \n      of all American soldiers from Mexico.<br>\n      <br>\n      Tensions increased between the two countries. Villa\'s forces attacked \n      another town in Texas. President Wilson considered asking Congress to \n      declare war. But the crisis cooled down before then. American forces were \n      withdrawn. And the people of Mexico elected a new government. They chose \n      Carranza as president.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      LARRY WEST: As President Wilson dealt with the situation in Mexico, \n      trouble began to surface in another part of the world. The crisis was in \n      Europe. Tensions were growing between several groups of nations. They were \n      on the edge of what would become World War One.<br>\n      <br>\n      The major powers in Europe had been threatening each other for years. But \n      they had not fought for more than forty years. Most Americans believed \n      there would never be another European war. Such a war would be \n      unbelievably destructive. Millions would die. No nation would win.<br>\n      <br>\n      MAURICE JOYCE: Europe depended on a balance of power to keep the peace. On \n      one side were the central powers -- Germany, Austria-Hungary, and Italy. \n      On the other side were the members of the triple entente -- Britain, \n      France, and Russia.<br>\n      <br>\n      Each side made every effort to win the support of Europe\'s smaller \n      nations. A number of nations refused to join either side. The neutrals \n      included Switzerland, Belgium, the Netherlands, and the Scandinavian \n      countries.<br>\n      <br>\n      LARRY WEST: This political balance did not prevent the major nations from \n      competing with each other for colonies and economic power. They competed \n      all over the world. In China, in the Middle East, in Africa -- everywhere \n      money could be invested. Competition was especially sharp in the Balkans. \n      This was the area of Europe between the Adriatic and Black Seas.<br>\n      <br>\n      Many nations claimed special interests in the Balkans. And several Balkan \n      countries were fighting each other. The whole continent seemed ready to \n      explode.<br>\n      <br>\n      MAURICE JOYCE: The spark that set off the explosion came in the city of \n      Sarajevo. The date was June twenty-eighth, nineteen fourteen. Sarajevo had \n      been taken over by Austria. And the Archduke of Austria -- Ferdinand -- \n      had come for a visit. Ferdinand was expected to become the next emperor of \n      Austria.<br>\n      <br>\n      Seven young extremists from the area decided to assassinate the Archduke \n      to protest Austrian control. One of the extremists threw a bomb at the \n      royal family. The bomb missed its target. But another extremist shot at \n      the group. He killed both the Archduke and the Archduke\'s wife.<br>\n      <br>\n      LARRY WEST: The assassinations in Sarajevo started a series of events that \n      quickly brought war to all of Europe. Soon the continent was covered with \n      armies, battles, and death. The war in Europe forced President Wilson to \n      face the greatest crisis of his presidency.<br>\n      <br>\n      That will be our story next week.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      BOB DOUGHTY: Our program was written by Frank Beardsley. Today, the United States Department of Agriculture’s \n      Agricultural Research Service operates the Arboretum.<br>\n      <br>\n      Its goal is to carry out studies and provide education in an effort to \n      improve the environment. Experts say teens are biologically \n      programmed to go to sleep later and wake up later than other age groups. \n      Yet many schools start classes as early as seven in the morning.<br>\n      <br>\n      As a result, many students go to class feeling like sixteen-year-old \n      Danny.
