It would be a flight around \n      the world without stopping or adding more fuel. Today, Frank Oliver and \n      Doug Johnson tell about a special plane called Voyager and the effort to \n      set a difficult world record.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      FRANK OLIVER: Voyager began as a quick drawing on a small piece of paper. \n      Six years later, the drawing was a plane that made history.<br>\n      <br>\n      Many people gave their time, energy and money to help make the flight \n      happen. But three people had lead parts in the event. Dick Rutan. Burt \n      Rutan. And Jeana Yeager.<br>\n      <br>\n      Dick Rutan was an experienced flier. He had been a pilot in the United \n      States military during the war in Vietnam. After the war, he worked as a \n      test pilot. He flew planes designed by his younger brother Burt.<br>\n      <br>\n      Burt Rutan was well-known as a designer of experimental planes. And Jeana \n      Yeager held nine world flight records as a pilot.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101013532751.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"113\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          Voyager \n          is now kept at the Smithsonian National Air and Space Museum in \n          Washington</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      DOUG JOHNSON: One day in \n      early nineteen eighty-one, Dick, Burt and Jeana were eating in a \n      restaurant in Mojave, California. Burt turned to his brother and asked a \n      wild question: &quot;How would you like to be the first person to fly around \n      the world without stopping to re-fuel?&quot;<br>\n      <br>\n      The three considered the idea. A non-stop flight around the world without \n      re-fueling was the last flight record to be set. The flight always had \n      been considered impossible. No plane could carry enough fuel to fly that \n      far: forty thousand kilometers.<br>\n      <br>\n      But now there were new materials for planes. Burt thought he could build a \n      plane that could make the voyage. Dick and Jeana thought they could fly \n      it. No one could think of a good reason not to try.<br>\n      <br>\n      Burt picked up a small piece of paper. He drew an airplane that looked \n      like a giant wing, and not much more. That was the beginning.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      FRANK OLIVER: Not since the days of Orville and Wilbur Wright had the \n      people making a record flight designed and built their own aircraft. Dick, \n      Burt and Jeana did. Some people thought their Voyager project was both \n      impossible and foolish. Everyone knew it would be dangerous.<br>\n      <br>\n      The Voyager crew worked on the plane in a small building at an airport in \n      California\'s Mojave Desert. Dick, Burt and Jeana received no government \n      money. Instead, they got small amounts of money from lots of different \n      people.<br>\n      <br>\n      As news of the project spread, more and more people offered to help. There \n      were aviation engineers and workers from the space agency\'s experimental \n      plane project. Several airplane companies offered equipment to be used in \n      the plane. When Voyager was finished, it had two million dollars\' worth of \n      parts in it.<br>\n      <br>\n      DOUG JOHNSON: Burt Rutan had built light-weight planes before. He knew a \n      normal plane made of aluminum metal could not make a trip around the world \n      without adding fuel. So his solution was to build Voyager almost \n      completely out of new materials. The materials were very light, but very \n      strong. This meant Voyager could lift and carry many times its weight in \n      fuel.<br>\n      <br>\n      The finished plane weighed just nine hundred kilograms, about the weight \n      of a small car. The full load of fuel weighed three times that much, about \n      three thousand kilograms. Voyager was not built to be a fast plane. It \n      flew about one hundred seventy-five kilometers an hour.<br>\n      <br>\n      FRANK OLIVER: The main wing of the finished plane was more than \n      thirty-three meters across. That is wider than the main wing on today\'s \n      big passenger planes. The center part of the plane held the crew. And on \n      either side of this body were two long fuel tanks.<br>\n      <br>\n      In fact, almost all of the Voyager was a fuel tank. Seventeen separate \n      containers were squeezed into every possible space. During the flight, the \n      pilots had to move fuel from container to container to keep the plane \n      balanced. One engine at each end of the body of the plane provided power.<br>\n      <br>\n      The area for the two pilots was unbelievably small. It was just one meter \n      wide by two-and-one-quarter meters long. The person flying the plane sat \n      in the pilot\'s seat. The other person had to lie down at all times.<br>\n      <br>\n      DOUG JOHNSON: After many test flights, the Voyager was finally ready in \n      December, nineteen eighty-six. The best weather for flying around the \n      world is from June to August. That time was far past. But the pilots were \n      tired of delays. They made the decision to Take off, knowing the weather \n      might be bad.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101013532752.jpg\" width=\"150\" height=\"183\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          Jeana \n          Yeager and Dick Rutan</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      On December fourteenth, Dick \n      Rutan and Jeana Yeager walked around the plane one more time. It looked \n      like a giant white flying insect. They were going to be trusting their \n      lives to this strange plane for the next nine days.<br>\n      <br>\n      Dick climbed into the only seat. Jeana lay on the floor. They were ready \n      to go. Flight controllers at Edwards Air Force Base in California cleared \n      them for a trip no one had ever attempted before.<br>\n      <br>\n      FRANK OLIVER: The long, thin wings of the plane were so loaded with fuel \n      that they almost touched the ground. Voyager began to move down the \n      runway, slowly. But something was wrong. The ends of the wings were not \n      lifting.<br>\n      <br>\n      Burt Rutan sent a radio message to his brother to lift the plane\'s nose. \n      &quot;Pull back on the stick!&quot; he screamed. &quot;Pull back!&quot; But Dick did not hear \n      the warning. And he did not see the wings. He was looking straight ahead.<br>\n      <br>\n      Voyager was getting dangerously close to the end of the runway. It \n      appeared about to crash. Finally, just in time, the long wings swept up. \n      The plane leaped into the air.<br>\n      <br>\n      Planes following Voyager could see that the ends of the wings were badly \n      damaged. Dick turned the plane so the force of air currents would break \n      off the broken ends. Then he aimed Voyager out over the Pacific Ocean.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      DOUG JOHNSON: Weight was the main consideration in designing the \n      experimental plane. Not safety. Not comfort. Voyager did not have most of \n      the normal safety equipment of modern planes. There were no special \n      materials to block the noise of the engines. And space for the pilots was \n      so tight they had great difficulty changing places.<br>\n      <br>\n      Voyager\'s long wings moved up and down as the winds changed. It seemed to \n      sail on waves of air, just like a sailboat on ocean waves. This motion \n      meant the flight was extremely rough.<br>\n      <br>\n      FRANK OLIVER: It was not an enjoyable trip. Dick and Jeana were always \n      tense. At the end of the second day, the weather expert for the flight \n      warned of trouble. Voyager was heading for an ocean storm. Dick was able \n      to fly close to the storm and ride its winds.<br>\n      <br>\n      On the third day, Voyager was in trouble again. It had to fly between huge \n      thunderhead clouds on one side and Vietnam\'s airspace on the other. Dick \n      was able to keep the plane safely in the middle.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101013532753.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          Voyager \n          in flight</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      Over Africa, the two pilots \n      struggled with continuous stormy weather. Dick had flown almost all of the \n      first sixty hours of the flight. Then he changed places with Jeana for \n      short periods. Both were extremely tired.<br>\n      <br>\n      Suddenly, a red warning light turned on. It was a signal that there was \n      not enough oil in one engine. Dick and Jeana had been so busy trying to \n      fly around bad weather and mountains that they had forgotten to watch the \n      oil level. But luck stayed with them.<br>\n      <br>\n      They added the necessary oil. The engine was not damaged.<br>\n      <br>\n      DOUG JOHNSON: Once past the violent weather over Africa, Dick and Jeana \n      began planning the way home. A computer confirmed that they had enough \n      fuel left to make it. But as they flew up the coast of Mexico, the engine \n      on the back of the plane failed. Fuel had stopped flowing to it.<br>\n      <br>\n      The more powerful front engine already had been shut down earlier to save \n      fuel. With neither engine working, Voyager quickly began to lose speed and \n      height. The plane fell for five minutes. Dick finally got the front engine \n      started again. Then fuel started flowing to the back engine, and it began \n      to work again, too.<br>\n      <br>\n      FRANK OLIVER: Nine days after take-off, Voyager landed smoothly at Edwards \n      Air Force Base in California. It had completed a Forty thousand kilometer \n      flight around the world. It had not stopped. And it had not re-fueled.<br>\n      <br>\n      Dick said after landing: &quot;This was the last major event of atmospheric \n      flight.&quot; Jeana added: &quot;It was a lot more difficult than we ever imagined.&quot;<br>\n      <br>\n      Burt Rutan\'s revolutionary plane design had worked. And, with it, Dick \n      Rutan and Jeana Yeager had joined the list of the world\'s greatest fliers.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: This Special English program was written by Marilyn Rice \n      Christiano. It was a \n      war no nation really wanted. But no nation seemed able to stop it.<br>\n      <br>\n      The assassination of Austria\'s Archduke Franz Ferdinand in the city of \n      Sarajevo was the spark that set off the explosion.<br>\n      <br>\n      This week in our series, Harry Monroe and Kay Gallant tell about the war \n      and how it affected the United States under President Woodrow Wilson.<br>\n      <br>\n      HARRY MONROE: The Austrian Archduke was murdered by Serbian nationalists. \n      They opposed Austrian control of their homeland. After the assassination, \n      Austria declared war on Serbia. One of Serbia\'s allies was Russia. Russia \n      agreed to help Serbia in any war against Austria.<br>\n      <br>\n      Austria had allies, too. The most important was Germany. Germany wanted \n      Russia to stay out of the war. When Russia refused, Germany declared war \n      on Russia. Then Germany declared war on Russia\'s close ally, France. \n      Britain entered the war a few days later when Germany violated the \n      neutrality of Belgium.<br>\n      <br>\n      KAY GALLANT: One nation after another entered the conflict to protect its \n      friends or to honor its treaties. Within a week, most of Europe was at \n      war.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101013532538.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"113\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          British \n          forces in action during World War One</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      On one side were the Central \n      Powers: Germany and Austria-Hungary. On the other side were the Triple \n      Entente Allies: France, Britain, and Russia. Many other nations took \n      sides. Bulgaria and Turkey joined the Central Powers. Italy, Romania, \n      Portugal, and Greece joined the Allies.<br>\n      <br>\n      HARRY MONROE: The United States hoped to stay out of the war. President \n      Wilson immediately declared American neutrality. He said: &quot;It is a war \n      with which we have nothing to do, whose causes cannot touch us.&quot;<br>\n      <br>\n      Most Americans agreed with President Wilson. They did not want to get \n      involved in the fighting. However, many found it difficult to remain \n      neutral in their hearts. Some Americans had family roots in Germany. They \n      supported the Central Powers. A greater number of Americans had family \n      roots in Britain or France. They supported the Allies.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101013532539.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          An \n          abandoned British trench captured by the Germans. German soldiers are \n          on horseback in the background.</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      Yet the official American \n      policy was neutrality. The United States planned to continue to trade with \n      both sides.<br>\n      <br>\n      KAY GALLANT: Germany and Austria expected a quick victory in the war. They \n      were caught between two powerful enemies: Russia and France. But German \n      military leaders were not worried. They had a battle plan they were sure \n      would succeed.<br>\n      <br>\n      The German generals planned to strike quickly at France with most of the \n      German army. They expected to defeat France in a short time and then turn \n      to fight Russia. In this way, the German army would not have to fight both \n      enemies at the same time.<br>\n      <br>\n      HARRY MONROE: At first, the plan worked. Two million German soldiers swept \n      across Belgium and into France. They rushed forward toward Paris, hoping \n      for a fast victory. But the German commanders made a mistake. They pushed \n      their men too fast. When British and French forces struck back -- outside \n      Paris -- the tired and worn German soldiers could not hold their \n      positions.<br>\n      <br>\n      The battle was fierce and unbelievably bloody. In the end, the Germans \n      were forced to withdraw.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101013532540.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          A 1915 \n          poster showing a French soldier holding a grenade</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      The German withdrawal gave \n      the allies time to prepare strong defenses. There was no chance now for a \n      quick German victory. Instead, it would be a long war, with Germany and \n      Austria facing enemies on two sides. Britain and France were on the West. \n      Russia was on the East.<br>\n      <br>\n      KAY GALLANT: The Allies took immediate steps to reduce Germany\'s trade \n      with the rest of the world. The British navy began seizing war supplies \n      found on neutral ships sailing toward German ports. It then expanded its \n      efforts to block food exports to Germany.<br>\n      <br>\n      The blockade by Britain and the other allies was very successful. Germany \n      faced possible starvation. Its navy was not strong enough to break the \n      blockade with surface ships. Its only hope was to break the blockade with \n      another naval weapon: submarines.<br>\n      <br>\n      Germany announced that it would use its submarines to sink any ship that \n      came near the coast of Britain. The threat included ships from neutral \n      nations that tried to continue trading with the Allies.<br>\n      <br>\n      HARRY MONROE: The United States and other neutral nations immediately \n      protested the German announcement. They said it was a clear violation of \n      international law.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101013532541.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          A German \n          submarine said to be the one that sank the Lusitania</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      When a German submarine sank \n      a British ship in the Irish Sea, one of the victims was an American \n      citizen. A few weeks later, an American oil ship was damaged during a sea \n      battle between British navy ships and a German submarine. Then came the \n      most serious incident of all. It involved a British passenger ship called \n      the Lusitania.<br>\n      <br>\n      The Lusitania was sailing from New York City to Britain when it was \n      attacked by a German submarine. The Lusitania sank in eighteen minutes. \n      One thousand two hundred persons were killed. One hundred twenty-nine were \n      Americans.<br>\n      <br>\n      KAY GALLANT: The sinking of the Lusitania shocked and horrified the \n      American people. They called it mass murder. They turned against Germany. \n      President Wilson warned that he might declare war on Germany, if Germany \n      continued to sink civilian ships.<br>\n      <br>\n      Germany did not want war with the United States. It already faced a strong \n      fight against the European Allies. It promised not to sink any more \n      civilian ships without warning. And it offered regrets for the Lusitania \n      incident.<br>\n      <br>\n      HARRY MONROE: President Wilson accepted Germany\'s apology. Like most \n      Americans, he hoped to stay out of the bloody European struggle. And he \n      also knew that the record of the Allies was not completely clean.<br>\n      <br>\n      For example, he was troubled by reports of mass hunger in Germany. He and \n      other Americans felt the British food blockade was cruel. They also were \n      shocked by the way British forces brutally crushed a rebellion in Ireland \n      at the time.<br>\n      <br>\n      Most of all, the American people were sickened by reports of what was \n      happening on the battlefields of Europe. The armies were using poison gas \n      and other terrible weapons. Soldiers on both sides were dying by the \n      millions. The war had become a bloodbath.<br>\n　\n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          <img border=\"0\" src=\"http://www.guluwang.com/guluimage/2010101013532542.jpg\" width=\"180\" height=\"180\"></td>\n        \n        \n          <td width=\"100%\">\n          \n          President Woodrow Wilson in 1916</td>\n        \n      \n      <p style=\"margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0\">\n      KAY GALLANT: The United \n      States had a presidential election in nineteen sixteen. President Wilson \n      won the nomination of the Democratic Party to seek re-election. Democrats \n      around the country shouted their support with these words: &quot;He kept us out \n      of war!&quot; Wilson himself did not like the words. He felt it raised false \n      hopes. But people continued to say it, because they did not want war.<br>\n      <br>\n      HARRY MONROE: The Republican Party nominated Supreme Court Justice Charles \n      Evans Hughes as its candidate for president. Hughes was a moderate \n      Republican. He supported a number of social reforms.<br>\n      <br>\n      Like Wilson, Hughes promised to keep the United States neutral. However, \n      one of his supporters was former President Theodore Roosevelt. And \n      Roosevelt called for strong American policies that could lead to war. \n      Roosevelt\'s words led many Americans to see Wilson as the candidate of \n      peace....and Hughes as the candidate of war.<br>\n      <br>\n      KAY GALLANT: Voting in the presidential election was very close. At first, \n      it seemed Hughes had won. He went to bed on election night believing he \n      would be America\'s next president. But voting results later that night \n      confirmed Wilson as the winner. The election was so close the Republicans \n      did not accept defeat for two weeks.<br>\n      <br>\n      Woodrow Wilson had won another term. During that term, he would find it \n      increasingly difficult to honor the words of the campaign. Finally, he \n      would find it impossible. The United States entered World War One while \n      Woodrow Wilson was president.<br>\n      <br>\n      That will be our story next week.<br>\n      <br>\n      (MUSIC)<br>\n      <br>\n      BOB DOUGHTY: Our program was written by Frank Beardsley. The United States is one. Another is Finland, where Dr. Terho Heikkinen of the University of Turku led this new study.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\n&quot;The problem has been that there has been no clear data on the efficacy of the vaccine in the youngest children,&quot; says Heikkinen. &quot;And therefore, many countries, although they think that influenza is an important illness in young children, they have been reluctant in moving forward in vaccination until some convincing data are available.&quot;</p>\r\n<p>Heikkinen and his colleagues set out to provide that convincing data.</p>\r\n<p>Children in their study ranged from nine months to just over three years old. As a whole, those who were vaccinated were less likely to get the flu. That was especially true among the youngest, those under age two. In that group, 12 percent of those who did not get a vaccination came down with flu, but only four percent who were fully vaccinated got the disease.</p>\r\n<p>&quot;This is the first study that clearly shows that the inactivated vaccine that\'s available for young children these days is clearly effective, even in the age group younger than two years,&quot; he said.</p>\r\n<p>The results of vaccination are actually better than those numbers suggest. In the 2007-2008 flu season, when this study was conducted, the vaccine was designed to protect against two varieties of the flu, but it turned out to be largely ineffective against type B. Against type A influenza, 10 percent of the unvaccinated under-twos got sick, but only two percent of those who were fully vaccinated got the flu.</p>\r\n<p>Heikkinen does not say that young children should start getting flu shots. But he said his study - and one other study that examined the issue a few years ago - both concluded that flu vaccines can be effective and safe for this age group, so policy makers, doctors, and parents ought to at least think about it.</p>\r\n<p>&quot;I believe that the vaccine recommendations of young children will be at least reconsidered in all countries that do not vaccinate at this time.&quot;</p>\r\n<p>Terho Heikkinen\'s study is published online by the journal, The Lancet Infectious Diseases.<br />\r\n&nbsp;</p>','http://f1.hxen.com/m2/standard/2010/11/20101127h.mp3');

The idea is simple &ndash; as the two nations with over 90 percent of the world&rsquo;s nuclear weapons, the United States and Russia have a responsibility to work together to reduce our arsenals. And to ensure that our national security is protected, the United States has an interest in tracking Russia&rsquo;s nuclear arsenal through a verification effort that puts U.S. inspectors on the ground. As President Reagan said when he signed a nuclear arms treaty with the Soviet Union in 1987, &ldquo;Trust, but verify.&rdquo;</p>\r\n<p>That is precisely what the New START Treaty does. After nearly a full year of negotiations, we completed an agreement earlier this year that cuts by a third the number of long-range nuclear weapons and delivery vehicles that the United States and Russia can deploy, while ensuring that America retains a strong nuclear deterrent, and can put inspectors back on the ground in Russia.</p>\r\n<p>The Treaty also helped us reset our relations with Russia, which led to concrete benefits. For instance, Russia has been indispensable to our efforts to enforce strong sanctions on Iran, to secure loose nuclear material from terrorists, and to equip our troops in Afghanistan.</p>\r\n<p>All of this will be put to risk if the Senate does not pass the New START Treaty.</p>\r\n<p>Without ratification this year, the United States will have no inspectors on the ground, and no ability to verify Russian nuclear activities. So those who would block this treaty are breaking President Reagan&rsquo;s rule &ndash; they want to trust, but not verify.</p>\r\n<p>Without ratification, we put at risk the coalition that we have built to put pressure on Iran, and the transit route through Russia that we use to equip our troops in Afghanistan. And without ratification, we risk undoing decades of American leadership on nuclear security, and decades of bipartisanship on this issue. Our security and our position in the world are at stake.</p>\r\n<p>Indeed, since the Reagan years, every President has pursued a negotiated, verified, arms reduction treaty. And every time that these treaties have been reviewed by the Senate, they have passed with over 85 votes. Bipartisan support for New START could not be stronger. It has been endorsed by Republicans from the Reagan Administration and both Bush Administrations &ndash; including Colin Powell, George Shultz, Jim Baker, and Henry Kissinger. And it was approved by the Senate Foreign Relations Committee by a strong bipartisan vote of 14-4.</p>\r\n<p>Over the last several months, several questions have been asked about New START, and we have answered every single one. Some have asked whether it will limit our missile defense &ndash; it will not. Some, including Senator Jon Kyl, have asked that we modernize our nuclear infrastructure for the 21st century &ndash; we are doing so, and plan to invest at least $85 billion in that effort over the next ten years &ndash; a significant increase from the Bush Administration.</p>\r\n<p>Finally, some make no argument against the Treaty &ndash; they just ask for more time. But remember this: it has already been 11 months since we&rsquo;ve had inspectors in Russia, and every day that goes by without ratification is a day that we lose confidence in our understanding of Russia&rsquo;s nuclear weapons. If the Senate doesn&rsquo;t act this year &ndash; after six months, 18 hearings, and nearly a thousand questions answered &ndash; it would have to start over from scratch in January.</p>\r\n<p>The choice is clear: a failure to ratify New START would be a dangerous gamble with America&rsquo;s national security, setting back our understanding of Russia&rsquo;s nuclear weapons, as well as our leadership in the world. That is not what the American people sent us to Washington to do.</p>\r\n<p>There is enough gridlock, enough bickering. If there is one issue that should unite us &ndash; as Republicans and Democrats &ndash; it should be our national security.</p>\r\n<p>Some things are bigger than politics. And if the Senate passes this treaty, it will not be an achievement for Democrats or Republicans &ndash; it will be a win for America.</p>\r\n<p>Thanks.&nbsp;</p>\r\n</div>','http://down02.putclub.com/virtual/backup/update/bush/putclub.com_2010obm1120.mp3');

INSERT INTO `dede_addonmusic` VALUES('672','57','','','119.6.30.70','','','<p>I\'m Susan Clark with the Special English program WORDS AND THEIR STORIES.</p>\r\n<p>In the nineteen thirties, a song, &quot;Brother, Can You Spare a Dime?,&quot; was very popular in the United States. It was the time of the big Depression. The song had meaning for many people who had lost their jobs.</p>\r\n<p>A dime is a piece of money whose value is one-tenth of a dollar. Today, a dime does not buy much. But it was different in the nineteen thirties. A dime sometimes meant the difference between eating and starving.</p>\r\n<p>The American economy today is much better. Yet, many workers are concerned about losing their jobs as companies re-organize.</p>\r\n<p>Americans have special ways of talking about economic troubles. People in businesses may say they feel the pinch. Or they may say they are up against it. Or, if things are really bad, they may say they have to throw in the towel.</p>\r\n<p>A pinch is painful pressure. To feel the pinch is to suffer painful pressure involving money.</p>\r\n<p>The expression, feel the pinch, has been used since the sixteenth century. The famous English writer William Shakespeare wrote something very close to this in his great play &quot;King Lear.&quot;</p>\r\n<p>King Lear says he would accept necessity\'s sharp pinch. He means he would have to do without many of the things he always had.</p>\r\n<p>Much later, the Times of London newspaper used the expression about bad economic times during the eighteen sixties. It said, &quot;so much money having been spent ... All classes felt the pinch.&quot;</p>\r\n<p>Worse than feeling the pinch is being up against it. The saying means to be in a lot of trouble.</p>\r\n<p>Word expert James Rogers says the word &quot;it&quot; in the saying can mean any and all difficulties. He says the saying became popular in the United States and Canada in the late nineteenth century. Writer George Ade used it in a book called &quot;Artie.&quot; He wrote, &quot;I saw I was up against it.&quot;</p>\r\n<p>Sometimes a business that is up against it will have to throw in the towel. This means to accept defeat or surrender.</p>\r\n<p>Throwing in the towel may mean that a company will have to declare bankruptcy. The company will have to take legal steps to let people know it has no money to pay its debts.</p>\r\n<p>Word expert Charles Funk says an eighteen seventy-four publication called the Slang Dictionary explains throwing in the towel. It says the words probably came from the sport of boxing, or prizefighting. The book says the saying began because a competitor\'s face was cleaned with a cloth towel or other material. When a boxer\'s towel was thrown, it meant he was admitting defeat.</p>\r\n<p>Most businesses do not throw in the towel. They just re-organize so they can compete better.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\n&nbsp;</p>','http://www.hxen.com/englishlistening/voaenglish/voaspecialenglish/2010-11-21/127751.html');

Women wore short skirts.</p>\r\n<p>Money differences between one group of Americans and another had become sharper at the beginning of the twentieth century.</p>\r\n<p>By the nineteen twenties, many people believed that gaining the material things one desired could bring happiness. F. Scott Fitzgerald wrote about the lives of people who lived as if that were true.</p>\r\n<p>SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: There was more to Fitzgerald than a desire for material things. &quot;The test of a first-rate intelligence,&quot; he said, &quot;is the ability to hold two opposed ideas in the mind at the same time, and still have the ability to act.&quot; His two opposing ideas involved seeking happiness from material things, and knowing that material things only brought unhappiness.</p>\r\n<p>Of his own time, he said: &quot;There seemed no question about what was going to happen. America was going on the greatest party in its history and there was going to be plenty to tell about.&quot; Yet if he described only the party, his writings would have been forgotten when the party ended.</p>\r\n<p>&quot;All the stories that came into my head,&quot; he said, &quot;had a touch of unhappiness in them. The lovely young women in my stories were ruined; the diamond mountains exploded. In life these things had not happened yet. But I was sure that living was not the careless business that people thought. &quot;</p>\r\n<p>Fitzgerald was able to experience the wild living of the period yet write about its effect on people as though he were just an observer. That is a major reason his writings still are popular.</p>\r\n<p>(Music)</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nSTEVE EMBER: Francis Scott Key Fitzgerald was born in the middle western city of Saint Paul, Minnesota. He grew up there. In his mother\'s family there were southern landowners and politicians. The member of the family for whom he was named had written the words to &quot;The Star-Spangled Banner,&quot; America\'s national song.</p>\r\n<p>His father was a businessman who did not do well. Scott went to free public schools and, when he was fifteen, a costly private school where he learned how the rich lived. <br />\r\nWhen F. Scott Fitzgerald was seventeen, he entered Princeton University.</p>\r\n<p>SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: Fitzgerald was not a good student. He spent more time writing for school plays and magazines at Princeton than studying. His poor record troubled him less than the fact that he was not a good enough athlete to be on the university\'s football team.</p>\r\n<p>University officials warned him he had to do better in his studies or he would be expelled. So he decided to leave the university after three years to join the United States Army. It was World War One, but the war ended before he saw active duty.</p>\r\n<p>He met his future wife while he was at one of the bases where he trained. The girl, Zelda Sayre, was a local beauty in the southern city of Montgomery, Alabama. She and Fitzgerald agreed to marry. Then she rejected him when her family said that Fitzgerald could not give her the life she expected.</p>\r\n<p>STEVE EMBER: Fitzgerald was crushed. He went to New York City in nineteen nineteen with two goals. One was to make a lot of money. The other was to win the girl he loved.</p>\r\n<p>He rewrote and completed a novel that he had started in college. The book, &ldquo;This Side of Paradise,&rdquo; was published in nineteen twenty. It was an immediate success.</p>\r\n<p>Fitzgerald told his publisher that he did not expect more than twenty thousand copies of the book to be sold. The publisher laughed and said five thousand copies of a first novel would be very good. Within one week, however, twenty thousand copies of the book were sold.</p>\r\n<p>At twenty-four, Fitzgerald was famous and rich. A week after the novel appeared, Scott and Zelda were married. F. Scott Fitzgerald had gained the two goals he had set for himself. <br />\r\nAt this point the fairy tale should end with the expression: &quot;They lived happily ever after.&quot; But that was not to be the ending for the Fitzgeralds.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nSHIRLEY GRIFFITH: Fitzgerald is reported to have said to his friend, the American writer Ernest Hemingway: &quot;The very rich are different from you and me.&quot; Hemingway is reported to have answered: &ldquo;Yes, they have more money.&quot; The exchange tells a great deal about each writer. Hemingway saw a democratic world where people were measured by their ability, not by what they owned.</p>\r\n<p>Fitzgerald saw the deep differences between groups of people that money creates. He decided to be among the rich.</p>\r\n<p>To do this he sold short stories to magazines and, when he had time, continued to write novels. He also continued to live as though his life was one long party.</p>\r\n<p>For several years he was successful at everything. Editors paid more for a story by Fitzgerald than by any other writer. And he sold everything he wrote. Some stories were very good. He wrote very fast, though. So some stories were bad. Even the bad ones, however, had a spirit and a life that belonged to Fitzgerald. As soon as he had enough good stories, he collected them in a book.</p>\r\n<p>STEVE EMBER: Fitzgerald quickly learned that a life of partying all the time did not help him write his best. But he could not give up the fun.</p>\r\n<p>Scott and Zelda lived in New York City. He drank too much. She spent too much money. He promised himself to live a less costly life. Always, however, he spent more than he earned from writing.</p>\r\n<p>In addition to the individual stories, two collections of his stories, &ldquo;Flappers and Philosophers,&rdquo; and &ldquo;Tales of the Jazz Age,&rdquo; appeared in nineteen twenty and nineteen twenty-two. A second novel, &ldquo;The Beautiful and Damned,&rdquo; also was published in nineteen twenty-two.</p>\r\n<p>SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: The novel was well received, but it was nothing like the success of his first novel.</p>\r\n<p>Fitzgerald was unhappy with the critics and unhappy with the money the book earned. He and his wife moved to France with their baby daughter. They made many friends among the Americans who had fled to Paris. But they failed to cut their living costs. Fitzgerald was always in debt. He owed money to his publisher and the man who helped to sell his writings. In his stories he says repeatedly that no one can have everything. He seemed to try, though. It looked for a brief time like he might succeed.</p>\r\n<p>STEVE EMBER: Fitzgerald continued to be affected by the problems that would finally kill him -- the drinking and the debts. Yet by nineteen twenty-five his best novel, &ldquo;The Great Gatsby,&rdquo; was published.</p>\r\n<p>It is the story of a young man\'s search for his idea of love. It also is a story of what the young man must do to win that love before he discovers that it is not worth having. <br />\r\nNext week we shall discuss this important novel. And we shall tell you about the rest of Fitzgerald\'s short life.</p>','http://s5.hxen.com/m2/voa/2010/11/20101121a.mp3');

He called it the League of Nations. To Wilson, the league was more important than any other part of the treaty.</p>\r\n<p>Not all Americans shared Wilson\'s opinion. Many feared the league would take away the power of the American government to declare war and make treaties. They also agreed with the leaders of the other allied nations. Establishing the league was less important than punishing the defeated enemy.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\n&nbsp;<br />\r\nLeft to right: Britain\'s Prime Minister David Lloyd George, Italy\'s Premier Vittorio Orlando, France\'s Premier Georges Clemenceau, and President Woodrow Wilson on June 28, 1919.</p>\r\n<p>LARRY WEST: The other major allied leaders at the peace conference were Prime Minister David Lloyd-George of Britain, Premier Georges Clemenceau of France, and Premier Vittorio Otto of Italy.</p>\r\n<p>Lloyd-George, Clemenceau, and Otto understood how much Wilson wanted the League of Nations. They used this knowledge to win Wilson\'s approval for other parts of the peace treaty.</p>\r\n<p>Wilson soon learned that, to get the league, he had to compromise on many issues. For example, he had to accept British and French demands to make Germany pay all war damages. The payments added up to more than three hundred thousand million dollars. Wilson also had to accept the allied takeover of Germany\'s colonies.</p>\r\n<p>SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: Some of Wilson\'s compromises violated his belief in self-determination. This was the right of all people to decide for themselves who would govern them.</p>\r\n<p>One compromise, for example, gave to Japan Germany\'s colonial rights in the Shantung area of China. China protested the decision. It asked that control of Shantung be returned to the Chinese government. But President Wilson needed Japan\'s support for the League of Nations. So he accepted Japan\'s demand for control of Shantung.</p>\r\n<p>There were other violations of the policy of self-determination. These affected the people and land along the borders of several European nations.</p>\r\n<p>For example, three million Germans were made citizens of the new nation of Czechoslovakia. Millions of other Germans were forced into the newly formed nation of Poland. And Italy received territory that had belonged to Austria.</p>\r\n<p>LARRY WEST: Today, most history experts agree Woodrow Wilson was correct in opposing these decisions. They say Germany\'s loss of territory and citizens caused deep bitterness. And the bitterness helped lead to the rise of fascist dictator Adolph Hitler in the nineteen thirties.</p>\r\n<p>In East Asia, Japanese control over parts of China created serious tensions. Both decisions helped plant the seeds for the bloody harvest of World War Two twenty years later. But allied leaders at the Paris peace conference were not looking far into the future. As one person said at the time: &quot;They divided Europe like people cutting up a tasty pie.&quot;</p>\r\n<p>SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: After months of negotiations, the peace treaty was completed. The Allies gave it to a German delegation on May seventh, nineteen-nineteen. The head of the delegation objected immediately. He said the treaty was unfair. He urged his government not to sign it.</p>\r\n<p>At first, Germany did not sign. The leader of the government refused and resigned in protest. But a new government was formed. And its leader signed the document at a ceremony at the palace in Versailles outside Paris.</p>\r\n<p>Finally, World War One was officially over.</p>\r\n<p>LARRY WEST: President Woodrow Wilson returned to the United States after the treaty signing ceremony. He was not completely satisfied with the treaty. Yet he believed it was still valuable, because it established the League of Nations.</p>\r\n<p>Wilson\'s battle for the league was only half over when the treaty was signed in Europe. He had to win approval from the United States Senate. That half of the battle would not be easy.</p>\r\n<p>SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: Part of the problem was political. Wilson was a member of the Democratic Party. The Senate was controlled by the Republican Party. Also, Wilson had refused to name any important Republicans to his negotiating team at the peace conference.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\n&nbsp;<br />\r\nHenry Cabot Lodge</p>\r\n<p>Part of the problem was personal. A number of senators disliked Wilson. One was Republican Senator Henry Cabot Lodge. Lodge was the powerful chairman of the Senate Foreign Relations Committee. He told a friend he never expected to hate anyone as much as he hated Wilson.</p>\r\n<p>LARRY WEST: Wilson spoke before the Senate just two days after he returned from Europe. He urged it to approve the peace treaty.</p>\r\n<p>Wilson said: &quot;The united power of free nations must put a stop to aggression. And the world must be given peace. Shall we and any other free people refuse to accept this great duty? Dare we reject it and break the heart of the world? We cannot turn back. America shall show the way. The light streams upon the path ahead and nowhere else.&quot;</p>\r\n<p>SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: The Senate Foreign Relations Committee began hearings on the treaty. It heard a number of people who opposed the League of Nations. They said the league would destroy the freedom and independence of the United States.</p>\r\n<p>The committee completed its hearings and prepared a report for the full Senate. The report said the United States should reject the treaty, unless changes were made. The committee proposed almost forty changes.</p>\r\n<p>LARRY WEST: The committee\'s report was a blow to President Wilson both politically and personally. He had worked extremely hard to win Europe\'s support for the idea of a league of nations. Great crowds in Paris had cheered him and his idea. Now, the Senate of his own country was about to reject it.</p>\r\n<p>Wilson decided he must take his case out of the hands of the peoples\' representatives. He would take the case directly to the people themselves. He would build public support for the treaty. If enough citizens supported it, he believed, the Senate could not reject it.</p>\r\n<p>SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: President Wilson planned a speaking trip all across the country. His family and his doctor urged him not to go. They said he was still weak from a recent sickness. But Wilson refused the advice. He said the treaty was more important to him than his own life.</p>\r\n<p>The president left Washington in early September. He traveled in a special train.</p>\r\n<p>In city after city, he made speeches and rode in parades. He shook thousands of hands. At times, he suffered from a painful headache. But there was no time to rest.</p>\r\n<p>LARRY WEST: Everywhere Wilson stopped, he urged the people to support the League of Nations. It was, he said, the only hope for peace.</p>\r\n<p>In Boulder, Colorado, ten thousand people waited to hear him. By then, Wilson was extremely weak. He had to be helped up the steps of the building where he was to speak. He made the speech. He said he was working to honor the men who had died in the war. He said he was working for the children of the world.</p>\r\n<p>SHIRLEY GRIFFITH: Wilson put all his heart and energy into his speeches. And, as his family and doctor had warned, the pressure was too great. While in Wichita, Kansas, the pain in his head became terrible. He could not speak clearly. His face seemed frozen. A blood vessel had broken in his brain. Wilson had suffered a stroke.</p>\r\n<p>The president was forced to return to Washington. His condition got worse every day. Soon, he was unable to move.</p>\r\n<p>Woodrow Wilson would spend the rest of his presidency as a terribly sick man. He continued to hold on to his dreams of a League of Nations. But his dreams now filled a broken body.</p>\r\n<p>We will continue our story next week.</p>\r\n<p>(MUSIC)</p>\r\n<p>&nbsp;</p>\r\n<p>BOB DOUGHTY: Our program was written by Frank Beardsley. The narrators were Shirley Griffith and Larry West.</p>','http://s5.hxen.com/m2/voa/2010/11/20101111a.mp3');

It is the home of the United States Military Academy. Many people speak of the academy as simply &quot;West Point.&quot;</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nIt was formed in eighteen-oh-two when Thomas Jefferson was president. It is the oldest of the five military colleges in the United States. It also is the oldest continuously occupied military post in the United States. This four-year school has about four thousand four hundred students, or cadets. Both men and women study here before joining the United States Army. After graduation, they are given the rank of second lieutenant.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\n&nbsp;usma.gov</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nStudents in Grant Hall at West Point</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nIt is difficult to become a student at West Point. Only about fifteen percent of students who apply are accepted. High school seniors must have very good grades. They also must be nominated by a member of the United States Congress or by the president of the United States.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nEach year about one thousand new students are chosen. They do not have to pay for their education. All student costs are paid by the United States Army. But the students must agree to serve actively in the Army for at least five years after finishing their studies.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nWest Point also accepts a limited number of international students. After graduation they return to their home countries to serve in their country\'s army.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nCadets can take classes in more than forty subject areas. The most popular are foreign languages, management information systems, history, economics and mechanical engineering. The cadets are required to take physical fitness courses. Most of them are also members of sports teams.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nAn important part of a student\'s life at West Point concerns the honor code. INSERT INTO `dede_addonmusic` VALUES('777','58','','','119.6.30.70','','','<p>I\'m Susan Clark with the Special English program WORDS AND THEIR STORIES.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nIn the nineteen thirties, a song, &quot;Brother, Can You Spare a Dime?,&quot; was very popular in the United States. It was the time of the big Depression. The song had meaning for many people who had lost their jobs.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nA <span style=\"color: #ff0000\"><strong>dime</strong></span> is a piece of money whose value is one-tenth of a dollar. Today, a dime does not buy much. But it was different in the nineteen thirties. A dime sometimes meant the difference between eating and starving.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nThe American economy today is much better. Yet, many workers are concerned about losing their jobs as companies re-organize.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nAmericans have special ways of talking about economic troubles. People in businesses may say they feel the pinch. Or they may say they are up against it. Or, if things are really bad, they may say they have to <span style=\"color: #ff0000\"><strong>throw in the towel</strong></span>.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nA <span style=\"color: #ff0000\"><strong>pinch</strong></span> is painful pressure. To feel the pinch is to suffer painful pressure involving money.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nThe expression, feel the pinch, has been used since the sixteenth century. The famous English writer William Shakespeare wrote something very close to this in his great play &quot;King Lear.&quot;</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nKing Lear says he would accept necessity\'s sharp pinch. He means he would have to do without many of the things he always had.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nMuch later, the Times of London newspaper used the expression about bad economic times during the eighteen sixties. It said, &quot;so much money having been spent ... All classes felt the pinch.&quot;</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nWorse than feeling the pinch is being up against it. The saying means to be in a lot of trouble.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nWord expert James Rogers says the word &quot;it&quot; in the saying can mean any and all difficulties. He says the saying became popular in the United States and Canada in the late nineteenth century. Writer George Ade used it in a book called &quot;Artie.&quot; He wrote, &quot;I saw I was up against it.&quot;</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nSometimes a business that is up against it will have to throw in the towel. This means to accept defeat or surrender.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nThrowing in the towel may mean that a company will have to declare bankruptcy. The company will have to take legal steps to let people know it has no money to pay its debts.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nWord expert Charles Funk says an eighteen seventy-four publication called the Slang Dictionary explains throwing in the towel. It says the words probably came from the sport of boxing, or prizefighting. The book says the saying began because a competitor\'s face was cleaned with a cloth towel or other material. When a boxer\'s towel was thrown, it meant he was admitting defeat.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nMost businesses do not throw in the towel. They just re-organize so they can compete better.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\n(MUSIC)</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nThis WORDS AND THEIR STORIES was written by Jeri Watson. I\'m Susan Clark.</p>\r\n<p>&nbsp;</p>','http://www.tingclass.com/plus/down.php?aid=208932');

The country turned away from the problems of Europe. Now it would deal with problems of its own.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nThis week in our series, Kay Gallant and MAURICE JOYCE: tell about the many changes in America during the early nineteen twenties.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nKAY GALLANT: There was a presidential election in America in nineteen twenty. President Woodrow Wilson was not a candidate. He had suffered a stroke and was too sick. The two major candidates were Democrat James Cox and Republican Warren Harding. Voters had a clear choice between the two candidates.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nCox supported the ideas of President Wilson. He believed the United States should take an active part in world affairs. Harding opposed the idea of internationalism. He believed the United States should worry only about events within its own borders.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nWarren Harding won the election. By their votes, Americans made clear they were tired of sacrificing lives and money to solve other people\'s problems. They just wanted to live their own lives and make their own country a better place.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nMAURICE JOYCE: This was a great change in the nation\'s thinking. For twenty years, since the beginning of the century, the United States had become more involved in international events.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nYoung Americans had grown up with presidents like Woodrow Wilson and Theodore Roosevelt. Both Wilson and Roosevelt had active foreign policies. Both helped start the nation on the road to becoming a major world power.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nThen came World War One. It was like a sharp needle that bursts a balloon. The United States and the Allies won the war against Germany and the Central Powers. But thousands of American troops had died in the European conflict. And many months were taken up by the bitter debate over the peace treaty and the League of Nations.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nMost Americans did not want to hear about Europe and international peace organizations any more.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nKAY GALLANT: Instead, Americans became more concerned with material things. During World War One, they had lived under many kinds of restrictions. The federal government had controlled railroads, shipping, and industrial production. At the end of the war, these controls were lifted. Industries that had been making war supplies began making products for a peacetime economy.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nWages for most workers in the United States were higher than ever at the beginning of the nineteen twenties. Men and women had enough money to enjoy life more than they had in the past.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\n(MUSIC)</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nMAURICE JOYCE: Technology made it possible for millions of people to improve their lives. It also caused great changes in American society. Two of the most important new technologies were automobiles and radio.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nIn the early years of the twentieth century, automobiles were very costly. Each one was built separately by a small team of skilled workers. Most Americans did not have the money to own an automobile.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nThen Henry Ford decided to make cars everyone could buy. He built them on an assembly line. Cars were put together, or assembled, as they moved slowly through the factory. Each worker did just one thing to the car before it moved on to the next worker. In this way, the Ford Motor Company could build cars more quickly and easily. And it could sell them for much less money.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\n&nbsp;<br />\r\nloc.gov</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nCranking a Model &quot;T&quot; Ford</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nKAY GALLANT: Before long, there were cars everywhere. All these cars created a need for better roads. Outside cities, most roads were made just of dirt. They were chokingly dusty in?dry weather and impassably muddy in the rain.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nThey were rough and full of holes. Few bridges connected roads across rivers and streams. America\'s new drivers demanded that these problems be fixed. So, local and state governments began building and improving roads as they had never done before.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nAs new roads were built, many new businesses opened along them. There were gasoline stations and auto repair shops, of course. But soon there were eating places and hotels where travelers could eat and sleep. In the nineteen-twenties, the United States was becoming a nation of car-lovers.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nMAURICE JOYCE: Cars changed more than the way Americans traveled. They changed the way Americans lived. They removed some of the limitations of living conditions.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nFor example, families with cars no longer had to live in noisy, crowded cities. They could live in suburbs -- the wide-open areas outside cities. They could use their car to drive to work in the city.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nBusinesses moved, too. No longer did they have to be close to railroad lines. With new cars and trucks, they could transport their goods where they wanted, when they wanted. They were no longer limited by train times.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nCars also made life on farms less lonely. It became much easier for farm families to go to town on business or to visit friends.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nKAY GALLANT: Cars helped Americans learn more about their nation. In the nineteen twenties, people could drive all across the land for not much money. Places that used to be days apart now seemed suddenly closer.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nFamilies that normally stayed home on weekends and holidays began to explore the country. They drove to the seashores and lakeshores. To the mountains and forests. To places of historical importance or natural beauty.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nMAURICE JOYCE: Not all the changes linked to the car were good, of course.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nAutomobile accidents became more common and deadly. Other forms of transportation, such as railroads, began to suffer from the competition. Some railroads had to close down. Horses and wagons -- once the most common form of transportation -- began to disappear from city streets.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nThere were not enough cars in the nineteen-twenties to cause severe air pollution. But the air was becoming less pure every year. And the roads were becoming more crowded and noisy.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\n(MUSIC)</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nKAY GALLANT: While the automobile greatly changed America\'s transportation, radio greatly changed its communication.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nThe first radio station opened in the state of Pennsylvania in nineteen-twenty. Within ten years, there were hundreds of others. There were more than thirteen million radio receivers. Most of the radio stations were owned by large broadcasting networks. These networks were able to broadcast the same program to stations all over the country.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nMAURICE JOYCE: Most programs were simple and entertaining. There were radio plays, comedy shows, and music programs. But there also were news reports and political events. Millions of people who never read newspapers now heard the news on radio. Citizens everywhere could hear the president\'s voice.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nLike the automobile, radio helped bring Americans together. They were able to share many of the same events and experiences.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nKAY GALLANT: Radio also was a great help to companies. Businesses could buy time on radio programs for advertisements. In these \'ads\', they told listeners about their products. They urged them to buy the products: cars. Electric refrigerators. Foods. Medicines. In this way, companies quickly and easily created a nationwide demand for their goods.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nAutomobiles and radios were not the only new technologies to change American life in the days after World War One. Still one more invention would have a great effect on how Americans spent their time and money. That was the motion picture.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nIt will be our story next week.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\n(MUSIC)</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nMARIO RITTER: Our program was written by Frank Beardsley. You can also follow us on Facebook and Twitter at VOA Learning English. Join us again next week for THE MAKING OF A NATION -- an American history series in VOA Special English.</p>\r\n<p>&nbsp;</p>','http://www.tingclass.com/plus/down.php?aid=209115');

Instead of taking a pill or getting a shot, patients would inhale their medicine to get the drug deep into the lungs.</p>\r\n<p>Tuberculosis is one of the world\'s most serious health challenges. The World Health Organization says one-third of the world\'s population is infected - that\'s two billion people. The vast majority of them never get sick, but last year alone more than nine million did, and an estimated 1.7 million died from TB.</p>\r\n<p>Treating tuberculosis has never been easy. Drugs must be taken for months, and too often patients stop taking the pills, which opens the way for resistant forms of the disease to develop. So the search is on for better ways to treat TB.</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\nA PhD student at the University of Colorado has been exploring using existing drugs in a new form - a very fine powder that is inhaled, instead of a pill. J\'aime Manion says most tuberculosis bacteria cluster in the lungs, so delivering drugs directly to the lungs can be more effective, faster, and require lower doses.</p>\r\n<p>&quot;Over and over again we see that when you treat with inhalation, you see a faster clearance of the bacteria,&quot; she said. &quot;And this is so important because the treatment times for tuberculosis are from three to six months, with tons of antibiotics, with terrible side effects.&quot;</p>\r\n<p>Manion has been working with inhalable particles that are about three microns in diameter - three one-thousandths of a millimeter across.</p>\r\n<p>Creating the inhalant from an established drug that\'s manufactured in pill form isn\'t simply a matter of grinding it up into a fine powder. Manion says she and her team found that adding an amino acid called leucine gave the powdered medicine some useful properties, such as keeping the individual particles from sticking together.</p>\r\n<p>&quot;And when they\'re less sticky, they\'re able to blow apart and disperse better. And that\'s a really big, important thing for inhalable antibiotics because we want them to not go in as a clump, but to go in as individual particles and to make the most of their small size, so that they get where they\'re going and they get to the deepest, smallest parts of the lungs,&quot; Manion explained.</p>\r\n<p>By inhaling the drug, the patient gets the medicine directly into the lungs, where most of the target tuberculosis bacteria are.</p>\r\n<p>&quot;The point of the inhalable antibiotics is, we can concentrate a dose in this area, and then maybe get more effect out of a lower dose because it\'s concentrated into a smaller area and it\'s hitting the affected site,&quot; she said. &quot;And after it hits its target, it then is absorbed by the bloodstream and does further good in the body.&quot;</p>\r\n<p>Inhaled drugs are already being used to treat other diseases, including asthma and cystic fibrosis. The challenges have mainly been in manufacturing the drug in such a way that the ultra-small particles actually get into the tiny passageways deep in the lungs. But scientists are overcoming some of those challenges.</p>\r\n<p>At the same joint meeting of the International Pharmaceutical Federation and the American Association of Pharmaceutical Scientists where J\'aime Manion presented her research, others were sharing their findings on inhalable drug delivery for conditions ranging from human papillomavirus to lung cancer.</p>\r\n<p>Manion\'s work so far has focused on developing an inhalable form of tuberculosis antibiotics. INSERT INTO `dede_addonmusic` VALUES('1171','63','','','119.6.30.71','','','<p>创作背景:</p>\r\n<p><br />\r\n背景简介<br />\r\n1992年，加拿大育空地方政府施行了一项名为「驯鹿增量」的计划，以变相扑杀狼群的方式，让原本因人类过度猎捕而数量锐减的驯鹿迅速繁殖。布列瑟农，那个安静的村庄，那个生长着温暖记忆的地方，顷刻间泊满离别的忧伤。这种起因于人却怪罪到狼的思考逻辑引发了马修&middot;连恩创作《狼》的动机。由马修&middot;连恩率领的30位音乐工作者，以音乐与人性记录了在原野上被人们大量屠杀的狼群的故事。耗时两年，以最直接的感情、最沉痛的呼吁，敲击着人们的心：育空河流域，狼群目睹着同伴断气在人类枪下的身影，它们的眼神中没有恐惧，只露出一股沉静，那是原野上的傲气天生的野性，在原野还能奔跑，血液尚未流尽之时，回首凝望，无法舔舐同伴的鲜血，就带着它的灵魂继续浪迹天涯. <br />\r\nbressanone收录于《狼》,马修自己写的关于这首歌的创作心得，这才是bressanone真正的创作背景，大致内容如下： <br />\r\n故事 <br />\r\n作者：马修&middot;连恩 <br />\r\n日期：2002年2月20日 18:10 <br />\r\n福利斯，你好 <br />\r\n谢谢你的评论，现在给你自己冲杯热茶，坐好，我接下来给你说个关于bressanone的故事...... <br />\r\n几年前，我疯狂地爱上了一个年轻的女孩，还有，也爱上了南部蒂罗尔山区，它在意大利的北部，与奥地利接壤，就在勃伦尔山脉的南边（勃伦尔山脉正好把意大利和奥地利分隔开来）。 <br />\r\n南部蒂罗尔曾经跟北部蒂罗尔（现在属于奥地利）和西部蒂罗尔（现在属于瑞士）是一个整体。这个地区的人说的是德国的一种方言，但是由于蒂罗尔被分割开来，而南部蒂罗尔变成意大利的一部分，所以这里的地名一般都有意大利文和德文两种名字。 <br />\r\n总之呢...许多年前我给绿色和平组织工作，在那时候我遇上了一个让我心动的女孩子。我们是在加州的约塞米蒂国家公园归途中相遇的。自那以后，她回到科罗拉多州的绿色和平组织，最后回到纽约州去上学，而我则回到圣迭戈的绿色和平组织，并且最后回到我在加拿大育空地区的老家。 <br />\r\n此后的几个月里我们不停地通讯。很快我们都希望能有更进一步的发展。她将要去意大利的佛罗伦萨学习艺术，我就要去德国的慕尼黑开始新的表演生活，跟一支叫&ldquo;三月粉&rdquo;的摇滚乐队...哈啊，没错，三月粉...那是另外一个故事了。 <br />\r\n当我俩都在欧洲的时候，我们选了一个处在佛罗伦萨和慕尼黑之间的地方约会。这就是南部蒂罗尔的一个小镇，德文里面它叫&ldquo;Brixen&rdquo;，意大利文就是&ldquo;bressanone&rdquo;。Bressanone是个非常优美的小镇。它被小乡村包围着，而山谷中回响着教堂的钟声，山羊在牧场漫步，远处是高耸的白色山头。 <br />\r\n我们在那里玩乐了几天，探索过周围的小乡村，还有彼此的心。离别的日子到了，她要回去的时候我陪着她去附近乡村的火车站，真是很令人沮丧啊，我们都要踏上各自的道路。流着泪水，我上了去火车站的公共汽车，在短短的40分钟路程里，我缓缓入睡了，在梦中，我隐隐约约地似乎听到了这样的一首歌，非常美妙的旋律和歌词。我醒来的时候，赶紧下了车，来到最近的咖啡店，把所听到的旋律和歌词写在一张餐巾纸上，好让我能够永远地记住它。 <br />\r\n一年以后，我才有机会把这首歌录下来。在我的心里，永远会留个地方是给她，还有那些小乡村，和这首歌。 <br />\r\n谢谢你这么认真地听我说。现在...是时候跟你同睡了，晚安!!! <br />\r\n:-) <br />\r\n马修 <br />\r\n附原文： <br />\r\nThe Story <br />\r\nby Matthew Lien <br />\r\nDate: 02-20-02 18:10 <br />\r\nHello Forrest, <br />\r\nThank you for your comments. Now make yourself a cup of hot tea, sit back, and I\'ll tell you a story about Bressanone... <br />\r\nSeveral years ago, I fell deeply in love with a young woman, and also with a part of northern Italy known as South Tyrol. It borders Austria, just south of the Brenner mountain pass which separates Italy from Austria. <br />\r\nSouth Tyrol used to be joined with North Tyrol (now a part of Austria) and West Tyrol (now a part of Switzerland). The people of this area speak a dialect of German. But since Tyrol was divided and South Tyrol became a part of Italy, all the place names are in both German and Italian. <br />\r\nAnyway... I once worked for Greenpeace many years ago, where I met a young woman who melted my heart. We met while on a retreat in California\'s Yosemite National Park. After the retreat, she returned to the Colorado Greenpeace office, and eventually went back to school in New York state. I returned to the San Diego Greenpeace Office, and eventually went back to my home in the Yukon, Canada. <br />\r\nOver the months we stayed in touch. Soon we discovered that we would both be close again. She was going to study art in Florence, Italy, and I was going to live in Munich, Germany performing with a rock band called &quot;Marching Powder&quot;... ahhhh yes, Marching Powder... but that\'s another story. <br />\r\nWhen we were both in Europe, we made arrangements to meet in a place that was close to being between Florence and Munich. This was the South Tyrolian town called &quot;Brixen&quot; in German, or &quot;Bressanone&quot; in Italian. Bressanone is a beautiful town surrounded by small villages high in the mountain valleys with churches ringing and sheep in the meadows, and the awesome peaks of the Dolomite mountains towering beyond. <br />\r\nWe spent several days exploring the mountain villages and each other\'s hearts. And when the day came for her to return, I took her to a train station in a nearby village, and we said goodbye. It was very sad to be going our own ways again. With tears in my eyes, I got on a bus and headed for the train station in Bressanone. During the short 40 minute bus ride, I fell asleep. And while I slept, I had a dream in which I could hear this song, complete with the words and music.
